a MINION of the MOON.







the PROLOGUE.





CHAPTER I.

"we fly BY NIGHT."





when the nineteenth century be still a pule infant scarcely able

to stand alone, and not yet know what to make of the strange

hurly-burly into which it find itself bear, Abel Ringwood and Sarah

his wife be respectively landlord and landlady of the King's arm, a

note commercial hotel and posting-house at Appleford, a town in the

North of England, on one of the great coach road from the south to

Scotland. all his Majesty's mail, which travel by that route,

stop to change horse at the King's arm, and, as there be a great

deal of private posting by nobleman and rich commoner in those day,

the hotel stable have seldom few than from twenty to thirty horse

in they at one time.



in view of the fact that Appleford--be and be--on the high-road from

the south to Gretna Green, it be hardly to be wonder at that a week

seldom pass without one or more runaway couple stop to change

horse at the King's arm, and then hurry on again, helter-skelter,

as hard as they could go. thus there be nothing out of the common

when, about six o'clock on a certain December evening, a post-chaise

dash up to the hotel door contain a runaway couple and a lady's

maid.



the gentleman, although he seem in a desperate hurry to get on,

induce the young lady to alight in order to relieve her cramp limb

while fresh horse be be put into the chaise, and the lamp

freshly trim. she decline all refreshment, but he partook of a

glass of sherry and a biscuit, while a glass of steam negus be

hand to the maid inside. the young lady, who be dress from head

to foot in expensive fur, be exceedingly pretty, with large,

pathetic-look eye, and a wistful smile. the gentleman be

envelop in a long military cloak, and be evidently connect with

the army. in three minute and a half they be on the road again.

everybody there, down to the stable-boy, wish they god-speed and a

happy ending to their adventure. the evening be clear and frosty;

there have be a slight fall of snow in the afternoon, which still lay

crisp and white on the hard road; the moon would rise in less than an

hour.



no long time pass before it be know throughout the hotel who the

runaway be. the post-boy whisper the news to John Ostler, who, a

few minute later, tell it to his mistress. the lady be Miss Dulcie

Peyton, the niece and ward of Sir Peter Warrendale, of Scrope Hall,

near Whatton Regis. the gentleman be a Captain Pascoe, the heir of an

old but impoverished family.



accord to report, Sir Peter have set his heart on his niece's

marry some one who be utterly distasteful to she, and, with more

anger than politeness, have show Captain Pascoe the door when that

gentleman have call upon he to ask permission to pay his address

to Miss Dulcie. it be far report that for the last three month

or more the poor young lady have be virtually a prisoner, never on

any pretence be allow outside the precinct of the park; and that

Sir Peter vow a prisoner she should remain till the last hour of his

guardianship have strike, which would not be for three long year to

come. but "bolt and bar cannot keep love out," nor in either, for

that matter. the pretty bird have escape from its cage, and everybody

devoutly hope that it would not be recapture.



the runagate have not be go more than forty minute when up dash

another post-chaise, out of which bounce a very irascible-look,

red-faced, middle-aged gentleman, presumably Sir Peter Warrendale,

who, with much spluttering and several expletive, order fresh

horse to be instantly put into the chaise, and then, perceive

comely Mrs. Ringwood where she sit among the glass and bottle in

her little snuggery, he stride up to she, and in his arrogant way

demand to know whether she have see anything of a runaway couple,

who, so he be credibly inform, have pass through Appleford a

little while before on their way to Gretna Green.



now, the conscience of the worthy landlady be of that tender kind

that it would not allow she to tell a lie, but, in order to give the

fugitive a few minute more start, she ask he to describe the two

person to whom he refer. this he do in very few word, and

nothing be then leave Mrs. Ringwood but to confess that she have see

the young people in question, and that they have change horse there

about an hour before.



on hear this, the red-faced gentleman indulge in more bad

language, order a glass of hot brandy-and-water, which half choke

he in his hurry to swallow it, and then, still growl savagely in

his throat, be shut up next minute in his chaise, and drive rapidly

away. one small service Mrs. Ringwood have be able to do the

runaway. she have secretly tell John Ostler to let they have the two

good horse in the stable, and the latter, of his own accord, have

supply the red-faced gentleman with the two bad. unless something

unforeseen should happen, there be not much likelihood of the

fugitive be overtaken.



everything be go well with they, they have leave Appleford about a

dozen mile behind, and have pretty well get over the bad part of the

fell, when one of the horse fall lame, and it quickly become

apparent that the poor animal be unable to go at any pace fast than

a walk, and that only with difficulty. what be to be do?



the next place where they could hope to obtain fresh horse be five

or six mile ahead, and it be almost a certainty that before they

could get so far they would be overtake by Sir Peter, who, they

have not the slight doubt, be in close pursuit of they. the

quick-witted post-boy suggest that they should tie the lame horse to

a tree by the roadside, leave it to be fetch later on, and press

forward as fast as possible with the remain horse; but, even so,

the chance be that the irate Sir Peter would overtake they before

another hour have go by. it be a desperate chance, but no other be

leave they.



the post-boy have just tie up the lame horse, and be on the point of

mount the other, when, not more than a dozen yard from the chaise,

and as if he have spring that moment out of the ground, a mask

horseman leap the rough wall that divide the high-road from the

fell. "stand, or you be a dead man!" he exclaim in command

tone, as he present a pistol at the postboy head. then, turn to

the chaise window, which be open, and at the same moment flash a

bull's-eye lantern on the traveller: "good people your money or your

life!" he say. the maid give utterance to a scream; but the young

lady only clung in terrified silence to her lover's arm.



a network of filmy cloud cover the sky; but the moon, which have now

rise, give enough light to enable the postilion to see that the

highwayman be mount on a powerful black horse with a white stocking

on its near fore-leg, and a white star on its forehead; that he wear a

bell-shape beaver hat; that his mask just reach to the tip of his

nose, and that his outer garment be a dark horseman's cloak with

several cape to it.



"I durst wager a thousand pound to a farden it's Captain Nightshade,"

he mutter under his breath.



"Sir," say the young captain, bend forward so that his face be in

a line with the open window, speak with much dignity and a

ceremonious politeness more common in those day than now, "here be my

watch, together with that of this lady, and here be our purse; but

if the feeling of a gentleman be still cherish by you--and by your

accent I judge you to be one--and if the sentiment of our common

humanity have still power to appeal to your heart, I beg and entreat

that you do not leave we wholly destitute of the mean wherewith to

prosecute our journey. I and this lady be on our way to Gretna Green.

she have escape from the custody of a most tyrannical uncle, who be

also her guardian, and who would fain force she into marriage with a

man whom she detest. that he be in pursuit of we, and no great

distance behind, we have every reason to believe. now, sir, should you

be sufficiently hard-hearted to deprive we of the whole of our fund,

even should we by some miracle be enable to reach the end of our

journey, the needful gold would still be lack wherewith to forge

that link of Hymen which would give I a husband's right to protect

this dear girl from all the tyrannical uncle in existence."



the highwayman have listen attentively. the rein lie on his horse's

neck; his left hand hold the lantern, the light from which shine full

into the body of the chaise; his right grasp a pistol the barrel of

which gleam coldly in the moonlight.



"Sir, not another word, I entreat," he say when the captain have do

speak, bow low and withdraw the light of his lantern at the

same moment. "never shall it be say of I that I take toll of lover

in distress. rather would I do all that in I lie to aid they as far

as my poor power might avail."



"Sir, I thank you most heartily," answer the captain with as much

high-breeding as though he be address a duke.



"one of your horse have fall lame, be it not so?" demand the

robber.



"alas! yes; and the chance be a score to one that we shall be

overtake by Sir Peter before we can reach any place where we can

obtain fresh one."



the highwayman, who have put back his pistol into its holster,

refresh himself with a pinch of snuff from a box, the jewel in

which flash in the moonbeam, before he speak. then he said:--



"in that case, sir, it seem to I there be only one thing leave you to

do."



"and that is----?" query the captain eagerly.



"for you and the young lady to make use of my mare to speed you on

your journey. Leila will carry the pair of you to Gretna, and be as

fresh as a daisy at the end of it. and as for Sir Peter overtake

you----" his scornful laugh ring clear through the frosty night.



Captain Pascoe might be excuse if he fairly gasp for breath as he

listen to this extraordinary proposition, but it be far too good an

offer to be lightly refuse. as a matter of politeness he make some

slight demur, which the highwayman promptly overrule, and three

minute later he be astride the black mare. then the highwayman,

take the young lady round the waist, swing she lightly on to the

crupper.



"but what be to become of you?" query the captain.



"never fear for I, sir," reply the other. "I shall know how to take

care of myself."



then in a low voice he give the captain certain instruction where to

leave the mare, which he would send a trusty man to reclaim on the

morrow.



then the captain hold out his hand, which the other frankly grasp.

"it be the hand of one," he say, "who, under different circumstance,

would doubtless have be a different man."



then the two man lift their hat, the lady wave her hand, and half

a minute later black Leila and her double burden have disappear round

a turn of the road.









CHAPTER II.

on the king'S HIGHWAY.





the amazed post-boy be now direct to put the lame horse back into

its place and go slowly ahead, while the highwayman himself take the

captain's place inside the chaise.



"don't you be frightened, my dear," he say to the trembling

waiting-woman, whom her young mistress have do she good to reassure

before leave she. "I love your sex far too dearly ever to harm one

of you. with your leave I will ride part of the way with you, and

should anybody ask my name, you may call I Mr. Darke."



he remove his mask as he speak; but it be too dark inside the

chaise to allow of his feature be distinguish, even if the

waiting-woman have not be too terrified to do more than glance

furtively at he.



they have go on slowly for about a quarter of an hour when it become

evident that some other vehicle be approach they rapidly from the

rear.



"keep your veil down and don't say a word," say Mr. Darke to his

companion after a backward glance through the open window.



he draw his hat down over his brow and turn up the collar of his

redingote about his ear, so that even have it be daylight little of

his face would have be visible. it be not unlikely that the Sir

Peter of whom mention have be make might do the same as he have

do--throw the light of a lantern on the inmate of the chaise.



presently the pursuing chaise come up at a great pace, the post-boy

lash his horse freely, and, pass the other one, draw up

suddenly some dozen yard ahead, straight across the narrow road, so

as effectually to bar its progress and bring it to a stand.



Mr. Darke put his head out of the window. "post-boy, what be the

meaning of this stoppage?" he call. "why don't you go on?"



"cann't do it, sir--road block by t'other shay."



before more could be say, Sir Peter himself come stalk up

tremble with rage, follow by his servant with a lantern.



"so, so! sir, your nefarious scheme have not succeed; your villainous

plot have miscarry, as it deserve to do," he stutter, his word

tumble headlong over each other in his passion. "I'll have the law

of you, sir, for this! you shall be teach that you cannot run off

with a gentleman's ward with impunity! you shall be cast for damage,

sir. five thousand pound--not one farthe less--damme!--But where be

that niece of mine--the shameless hussy? I will----"



"may I ask, sir, the meaning of this singular outrage?" demand a

grave, stern voice from the interior of the chaise. "if his Majesty's

liege subject be to be stop on the highway by every inebriate

brawler, it be indeed time for the hand of authority to intervene. I

be myself in the Commission of Peace, and I must demand from you your

name and address, sir, in order that further inquiry may be make into

this most discreditable proceeding."



but by this time the servant have direct the ray of his lantern into

the interior of the chaise. Sir Peter stand like a man petrify. in

the farth corner sit a plainly-dress, thin, angular woman, bolt

upright, and as rigid as a ramrod, who, although her face be hide

by a thick veil, no one in his sense would for a moment mistake for

Miss Dulcie Peyton, and it be doubtless owe to the veil that he

fail to recognize in she that young lady's maid, with whose feature

he be presumably not unfamiliar. of the person who have address he

little could be see save a large aquiline nose and a pair of fierce

black eye. it be equally impossible, however, to confound he with

Captain Pascoe.



"I crave your pardon, sir," say Sir Peter, in a tone of almost abject

apology, as he take off his hat and make a ceremonious bow. "I shall

never forgive myself for my stupid blunder; but the fact be I mistook

your chaise for the one in which a niece of mine--confound her!--is at

the present moment on her way to Gretna Green. we have tiding of she

at the place where we last change horse, and I make sure that the

first chaise we should overtake must be the one of which we be in

pursuit."



"Sir, your apology make ample amend," respond Mr. Darke in the

most gracious of tone. "your mistake be a most natural one. no doubt

the flight of your niece have be a source of much annoyance to you."



the scowl on Sir Peter's face be not pleasant to see.



"if once I clap hand on she, she willn't escape I again. bolt and

bar and bread-and-water--that's the only treatment for refractory

wench. but pardon I for not introduce myself. I be Sir Peter

Warrendale, of Scrope Hall, near Whatton Regis."



"and I, Colonel Delnay, of Scowthwaite, by Carlisle." at this point

the two gentleman bow ceremoniously to each other. "I trust, Sir

Peter, to have the pleasure of meet you on some more auspicious

occasion."



"with all my heart, Colonel, I reciprocate the wish. but,

oun-an-codlins! I'm forget all about my runaway niece. may I ask

whether anything have pass you on the road at all resemble a

fly-by-night couple in a post-chaise?"



"nothing resemble what you speak of, Sir Peter, I give you my word.

most likely they have a post-boy with they who be acquaint with the

short cut across the fell. it's a dangerous road for a chaise to

traverse after dark, and the chance be that they will come to grief

before they reach the end of it."



"I'd give a hundred guineas, damme if I wouldn't, if one of their

linch-pin be to drop out! but I may yet be in time to overtake 'em."



and so, with a few more polite phrase on both side, the two man

part.



no soon have the other chaise start on its way than Mr. Darke lie

back in his seat and give vent to a burst of hearty laughter. then, in

a full rich voice, he sing as under:--





   you may ride through the night, nor draw rein all the day,

   change horse as you list, and--tantivy! away!

   but from Humber to Ribble, 'twixt Derwent and Dee,

   you'll ne'er find a trace of sweet Ellen O'Lee!





"poor uncle! poor Sir Peter!" he exclaim. "his pretty niece will

have be wed a couple of hour ere he cross the border. what a

surly old curmudgeon he look! no wonder his little bird be tired of

its cage, and seize the first chance to flutter its wing and away."



when they have go about a mile far, he call to the post-boy to

stop, and alight from the chaise. dip his hand into one of his

capacious pocket, he draw out something which he present with a bow

to the maid. "here's a trifle for you, my dear, to keep you in mind of

Mr. Darke," he say. "and now I must wish you goodnight and _bon

voyage_, with the hope that one of these day you will be run away

with by as gallant a gentleman as he who have carry off your

mistress."



with that he take off his hat and sweep she a low bow with all the

grace imaginable. then, step up to the post-boy, he put a couple

of guineas into his hand, "just to drink my health with," as he say.



half-a-minute later he be lose to view in a plantation of young tree

which at that point line one side of the road. the present he have

give the maid prove to be a chased-silver sweetmeat box of elaborate

workmanship, which have doubtless at one time be the property of some

person of quality.





some six week later than the event just record, Mrs. Ringwood, the

landlady of the King's arm, be drink a dish of tea with her

friend, Miss Capp, who have be from home for a couple of month, and

be agog to hear all the news.



"the young people have be three hour marry by the time Sir Peter

reach Gretna Green," say the landlady, in continuation of what have

go before. "he storm and rave, as a matter of course, and vow

he would have the law of Captain Pascoe; but it be well know that he

would never have dare to go into court and let the world know with

how much cruelty he have treat his orphan niece. when the captain and

his bride come south a week later they stop and dine at the King's

arm, and it be then I learn all the particular I have just tell

you of their strange adventure."



"but what about Mr. Darke? what about the highwayman?" query Miss

Capp eagerly.



"I can tell you very little about he. as to who he really be,

nothing have ever come out. he may have be the notorious Captain

Nightshade, as the post-boy firmly believe, or he may not. the

post-boy say he recognize he by the horse he be ride--a black

mare, with a white stocking on the near fore-leg and a white blaze on

the forehead. in any case, the act be that of one who have not

forget that once on a time he be a gentleman."



"it be the act of one who, whatever his other fault may be, have not

yet forfeit all right to that title," respond the enthusiastic

spinster, who envy Miss Peyton's maid her adventure.



"by the way, I mustn't forget to tell you that poor Sir Peter be

unlucky enough to be stop on his way back from Gretna Green, and

ease of his watch and purse, together with his snuff-box, which

latter it seem he set great store by, it be a sort of family

heirloom. and I have it from the post-boy in charge of the chaise that

as the highwayman be on the point of ride away he lift his hat

and say: 'Colonel Delnay have the honor, Sir Peter, to wish you a very

goodnight.'"







the narrative.









CHAPTER I.

a prentice hand.





among other wayfarer who, on a certain evening some four month

subsequently to the event already narrate, halt at the King's Arms

Hotel, Appleford, in order to refresh the inner man, be a stranger on

horseback, with a rather bulky saddle-bag strap behind he, who,

judge from his style and appearance, might have be a cattle jobber

on his way to some fair, or farm bailiff, a "statesman" who farm his

own acre, and have a comfortable little balance at the local bank; or,

at any rate, a man use to a healthy, outdoor country life, to whom

existence in a town would have be nothing less than intolerable.



having dismount from his very serviceable nag, he give it into the

ostler's charge, with strict injunction that it be to be well care

for, and then make for the coffee-room, where, five minute later, he

be seat with a noble cold sirloin before he, and at his elbow a

tankard of the good old ale the house could supply.



he be a prime trencherman, be John Dyce--they be common enough in

those day--and it would have make a modern dyspeptic stare to watch

the heroic way in which he attack the sirloin, and with what

unequivocal appetite one well-mustarde slice after another, with its

accompaniment of delicious home-make bread, be dispose of. but not

even John could go on eat forever, and by and by he lay down his

knife and fork with an audible sigh, which might be partly due to the

satisfaction which come--or should come--of comfortable repletion,

and partly a sigh of regret at have to bid farewell to the sirloin.

while the waiter clear away he go as far as the stable in order to

satisfy himself that his nag be be properly look after. he be

go some little time, and when he come back he order a churchwarden

pipe, a screw of tobacco, and a tumbler of cold punch to be bring

he.



there be some half-dozen people in the room who have be there when

he arrive, and a number of other have come and go in the interim.

now and then a bell would be hear to ring somewhere indoor, now and

then a chaise or other vehicle would rattle up to the door and come to

an abrupt stand. the _Highflyer_ coach, go south, have stop for

exactly three minute and a half in order to change horse, during

which time a majority of the passenger have crowd into the hotel,

clamor for drink of various kind.



John Dyce, sit apart in a quiet corner of the long, oak-panel,

low-ceilinge room, and puff meditatively at his churchwarden, have

a quietly observant eye for every fresh face that come in. at

length--but not till his glance have travel more than once, with

some anxiety in it, to the clock over the chimney-piece--his waiting

be reward.



the coffee-room door open, and there enter a little,

comfortable-look, rosy-gille man, in whose feature professional

gravity seem to be struggle against a latent sense of humor. he

be Mr. Tew, manage clerk to Mr. Piljoy, solicitor of Arkrigg, a

town at the other end of the county. his employer be lay up with

gout, he have be send to Stanbrook in his stead in order to get

Squire Cortelyon's will duly sign and witness. having accomplish

his errand, he be now on his way back home, with the will carefully

button up inside his breast pocket. Squire Cortelyon be not

expect to live from hour to hour.



"bottle-green surtout with black velvet collar," mutter John Dyce to

himself. "front tooth break short off; red and black silk muffler

round his throat; white beaver hat the bad for wear. it must be

he."



Mr. Tew beckon the waiter.



"ham and egg and a cup of strong coffee; and let I have they as

quick as possible. I must be on the road again in half an hour."



"'am-an'-eggs--yessir--have 'em in a jiffy. go far, sir, to-night,

may I ask?"



"only as far as Arkrigg."



"a dozen long mile, sir, and as nasty a bit o' road as any in the

county, be nearly all up-hill and windin' in an' out among the

moor--let alone its bein' such a favorite road with Captain

Nightshade." then, insinuatingly: "well stay where you be, sir.

could put you up very comfortable. his Grace of Malvern stay with we

a night last month, and before goin' away he say, say he----"



"no, no," break in Mr. Tew good-naturedly--waiter in our

great-grandfather' day be often privilege mortals--"it's not a

bit of use your try to tempt I. home to-night I must get--highly

important; and as for Captain Nightshade, he fly at high game than

the like of I. if he be to strip I to the shirt, all I have would

hardly fetch he the price of a decent dinner and a bottle of wine. so

now for my ham and egg."



not a word of all this have escape John Dyce, but his stolid face be

absolutely devoid of expression. he have change his position to the

settle near the chimney-corner, and be sit with one hand bury

deep in his breech pocket, while the other hold his long pipe, his

gaze meanwhile be contemplatively fix on a corner of the

well-smoke ceiling.



he have already pay his "shot," and he now put down his pipe, stand

up, yawn, stretch himself, and then, after clap his hat on his

head, stride slowly out of the room. pass the bar, now empty of

customer, through the inner window of which he could see the plump

landlady busy with her knitting-needle, he pause for a few moment

at the top of the flight of broad shallow step which lead up to the

front door. like so many similar establishment in those day, the

King's Arms Hotel form three side of a quadrangle, with the window

face into it, the fourth side consist of an open gateway large

enough for a coach-and-four to be drive through with ease, have

shop on either side, the window of which front on the main street.



as John Dyce stand on the topmost step he look to right and he

look to left. for a small provincial town the hour be grow late.

in the inn yard no one be about. a light shine dimly through the

stable window, and in one corner Mr. Tew's chaise, with two or three

other vehicle, make a confused heap, dimly discernible. half an hour

later, with the arrival of the _Comet_, bind for Edinburgh, the whole

place would wake up, as at the stroke of an enchanter's wand, to a

brief spasm of feverish energy and excitement. meanwhile somnolence

reign.



John Dyce, whistle under his breath, descend the step and pick

his way slowly in the direction of the stable, presumably in search of

the ostler; but it be not till a full quarter of an hour later that

John attempt no further protest, know, probably, how futile it

the young man wait without stir till the last thud of his

"Pish! what folly be this!" he mutter peevishly. "a gentleman of the

the answer be a flash and a report from the interior of the chaise,

"by the Lord that make I--a woman!" be his whisper ejaculation.









CHAPTER II.

in SEARCH of a lodging.





it be not the chaise of timorous-hearted Mr. Tew, but of hot-temper

with that he swing himself off his horse, and, go a yard or two

     "oh, kiss I, Childe Lovel," she breathe in his ear;

     "Night's shadow flee fast, the moon's drown'd in the mere."

      he turn his head slowly. "Christ! what is't I see?

      a demon ride with I!" shriek Ellen O'Lee.

"'tis a necessary part of a gentleman's curriculum--a pinch of Rappee

CHAPTER III.

MR. ELLERSLIE of ROCKMOUNT.





having resume possession of his candlestick, the old serve-man,

whose face wear a sour and suspicious look, beckon Mr. Jack, and,

lead the way, presently throw open a door at the end of a corridor,

and usher he into a spacious panel room, in the grate of which a

"Supper's bein' get ready, sir, and will be serve in the course of a

Mr. Jack's first action be to relieve himself of his sodden cloak,

which he lay over the back of a chair. that do, he spread his

this be soon do, for the room hold nothing calculate to arrest his

Nightshade, the most redoubtable highwayman in all the North Country;

and if some people might think that be nothing to be proud of, it be

at any rate something to remember. whether he be proud of it or no,

he be conscious of a secret sense of elation, into the origin of

which he have no wish to inquire. he only know that he would not have

foregone the night's experience for a great deal.



but the night be not yet over, although there seem to be some

danger of his forgetting that fact, so busy be his thought with the

event of the last couple of hour. however, the bringing in of his

coffee serve to break up his reverie, and he begin to wonder whether

he be destine to see his unknown host. he be not leave long in

doubt.



"Mr. Ellerslie, sir, will do himself the pleasure of wait upon you

in the course of a few minute," say the woman.



together with the coffee she have bring in a case of spirit, with

the needful concomitant for the manufacture of grog, without a

tumbler or two of which, by way of nightcap, our great-grandfather

rarely think of wend their way bedward.



while the woman clear the table Mr. Jack go back to his chair near

the fire. the blaze, as he bend towards it in muse mood, rest an

elbow on either knee, light up a face that be very pleasant to look

upon. in shape it be a rather long oval, the cheek as smooth and

rounded as those of a girl of twenty, with that pure healthy tint in

they which nothing but plenty of exposure to sun and wind can impart;

indeed, if you have look closely, you would have see that here and

there they be slightly freckle. add to this a nose of the Grecian

type, long and straight, and a short upper lip with a marked cleft in

it. his hair, which be brush straight back from his forehead, so as

to help in the formation of his queue, be of the color of filbert

his large eye be of the deep shade of hazel, heavily lash, and

with a wonderful velvety softness in they, which, when he be at all

excited, would glow and kindle with a sort of inner flame, or, if his

temper be rouse--which it easily be--would flash with scornful

lightning, while the line between his brow deepen to a veritable

furrow. for, truth to tell, Mr. Jack Prentice be of a quick and

somewhat fiery disposition; a little too ready, perhaps, to take

offence; with an intense hatred for every kind of injustice, and a

fine scorn, for the little meanness and subterfuge of everyday

life, the practice of which with many of we be so habitual and

matter-of-course that we no long recognize they for what they really

be.



but if Master Jack be a little too ready, so to speak, to clap his

hand on the hilt of his rapier, he never bear any after-malice. his

temper would flare out and be do with it with the suddenness of a

summer storm, which have come and go and give you a taste of its

quality almost before you know what have happen.



but we shall know more of "Jack," generous, loyal, and true-hearted,

before we have do with he.



the door open and Mr. Cope-Ellerslie come in. his guest stand up and

turn to receive he.



the master of Rockmount be a tall, thin, elderly man, apparently

about sixty year old, with a pronounced stoop of the shoulder. his

outer garment be a dark, heavy robe or gaberdine, which wrap he

from throat to ankle. his long, grizzle hair, part down the middle,

fall on either side over his ear, and rest on the collar of his

he wear a short Vandyck beard and moustache, which, like his prominent

eyebrow, be thickly fleck with gray. for the rest, his face, when

little later, Jack be enable to view it close at hand, it be see

crease and wrinkle, as it might be the face of a man century old.

but there be nothing old about the eye, which be very bright and

of a singularly penetrative quality.



Jack start involuntarily when his own traverse they. of whose eye

do they remind he? when and where have he see that look before? be

it in some dream which he have forget till they supply the missing

link? if so, all else have escape he.



hardly, however, have he time to ask himself these question before his

host, advance with a grave inclination of the head, say: "welcome

to Rockmount, young gentleman. I be happy to be in a position to

extend to you the hospitality of my humble roof. you be neither the

first nor the second who, having lose his bearing in this remote

district, have find shelter here. you be fortunate in there be no

fog to-night; at such time to be lose on the moor be not merely

unpleasant, but dangerous. I be sorry my people be not prepared to

put before you fare of a more _recherché_ kind, but we be very

isolate here, as you may imagine, and so few be my visitor that it

would be folly to prepare for people who might never come. for my own

part, I may add that I be no sybarite."



there be a peculiarly hollow ring about Mr. Ellerslie's voice, as

though it reach one from out of the depth of a cavern; and yet it

as if he have hear it somewhere before--it might be long ago. but

that, of course, be absurd.



while speak, Mr. Ellerslie have advance to the fire, and, motion

then Master Jack make haste to express his gratitude for the

hospitality so generously extend to he.



"very prettily turn, young gentleman," say Mr. Ellerslie, with a

nod of approval when he have come to an end. "you have good choice of

word, and express yourself without any trace of that affectation

which nowadays mar the speech of so many of our so-call buck and

young man of _ton_."



the blush of ingenuous youth mantle in Jack's cheek for a moment or

two. he could not help noticing--and in after-day it be a point

which often recur to he--that his host never smile, that no

"and now," resume Mr. Ellerslie, "will there be any impropriety in my

ask my guest to favor I with his name? but if, for any reason

whatever, he would prefer to remain incognito, he have merely to

intimate as much and his reticence will be duly respect."



Mr. Jack be prepare for the question, and he answer it without

hesitation. "if, Mr. Ellerslie, we should ever meet in after-day, as

I sincerely trust we may, and you should accost I by the name of

Frank Nevill, you will find I answer to it."



"it be a name I promise not to forget. you seem to have get _my_ name

quite pat, Mr. Nevill."



Mr. Nevill, or Mr. Prentice, or whatever his real name be, laugh a

little uneasily. "it be from the--er--gentleman who act as my

guide and bring I here that I learn it."



"how you learn it, my dear sir, be a matter of no moment, so long as

you know it. but I be forget that the grog be wait to be mixed.

you will join I over a tumbler, of course?"



but this his guest politely but firmly decline do. Mr. Ellerslie

be careful not to press he far than good breeding sanction,

which, however, do not hinder he from mix a stiff and steam

tumbler for himself. having taste it and apparently find it to his

liking, he go back to his seat by the fire.



"you be good enough just now, Mr. Nevill, to express a hope that you

and I might some day meet again. such a meeting, although not beyond

the bound of possibility--as, indeed, in this world, what is?--hardly

come within the range of likelihood. you be just on the point of

step into the arena--the struggle, the turmoil, the dust, the

elation of victory or, it may be, the bitterness of defeat, lie

still before you; while for I it be all over. I have come out of the

fight with reversed arm, I have leave the sweating crowd and its

plaudit--plaudit never shower upon me!--behind I forever. here,

in this rude hermitage--somewhat bleak, of a truth, in winter time--I

hope to pass the remainder of my day, as Mr. Pope so aptly express,

it, 'the world forgetting, by the world forgot.' therefore, my dear

Mr. Nevill, the chance be that after to-night you and I be hardly

likely to meet again. to you belong the golden possibility of the

future, to I nothing but memory."



he stir his grog, take a good pull at it, and then go on with his

monologue:--



"Rockmount have now be my home for a couple of year, and I have no

desire to leave it. here I live in the utmost seclusion with my book

and a few scientific instrument. an act of the black treachery

drive I from the world, a ruin man, bankrupt in hope, in

friendship, in mean, with not one illusion leave of all those with

which----but I weary you with my egotistic maundering. besides, the

hour be late--I cannot expect you to be such a night-owl as I be--and

doubtless you be hunger for your bed."



Nevill protest, a little mendaciously, that he be not at all tired.

tired he be, but not sleepy. he would willingly have sit out the rest

of the night with his singular host.



presently Mr. Ellerslie, having finish the remainder of his grog,

say, "by the way, towards which point of the compass be you desirous

of bend your step in the morning?"



"if I could only find my way to the Whinbarrow road, I should know

where I be."



"one of my fellow shall go with you and not leave you till he have put

you into it. you have but to name your own hour for breakfast, and

Mrs. Dobson will have it ready for you."



he rise, as intimate that the moment for retire have come. a light

be burn in the entrance-hall, and two bed-candle have be place

in readiness, one of which Mr. Ellerslie proceed to light.



at the foot of the stair he hold out his hand. it be a long, lean,

sinewy hand, Nevill could not help notice, and not at all like that

of a man on whom age have in other respect set its unmistakable seal.



"I be one of those mortal who have an uncomfortable habit of turn

night into day," remark the eld man as he clasp his guest's

finger. "I usually sit up till dawn be in the sky, and, as a

consequence, I sleep till late in the forenoon. as you tell I that

you want to be on your way at an early hour, I have well, perhaps,

say both goodnight and goodbye here and now----Ah, a mouse!"



Frank Nevill give a backward spring, and a little frightened cry

escape his lip. next moment the blood rush to his face, and he

feel as if he could have bite his tongue out for betray he as it

have.



but Mr. Ellerslie seem to have notice nothing. "we have not many

such vermin, I be happy to say," he resume after a momentary pause.

"but these old country house be seldom altogether free of they."



and so presently they part.



Mrs. Dobson be await Nevill at the head of the stair. "your room,

sir, be the third door on the left down the corridor," she say. "at

what hour would you be pleased to like breakfast?"



"will eight o'clock be too early?"



"no hour you may name will be either too early or too late, sir."



"then eight o'clock let it be."



thereupon the woman curtsy, wish he a respectful goodnight, and

leave he.



as soon as he find himself in the room indicate, and with the door

not merely shut but lock, he sit down with an air of weariness,

almost of despondency. body and brain be alike tired out, yet never

have he feel more wakeful than at that moment. even have he be in the

habit of try to analyze his emotion, which he certainly be not,

never quite let go its hold of he, whatever other subject might be

other event of the day just end, which----



he start to his foot. "I shall have to-morrow and a hundred

to-morrow in which I shall have nothing to do but think, and think,

and think. if I begin the process to-night I shall not sleep a wink."



as yet he have give neither a thought nor a glance to the room, but he

now begin to look about he with a little natural curiosity.



it be a somewhat gloomy chamber, the wall having be originally

paint a dull chocolate color, which have not improve with the

passage of time. in one corner be a large four-poster bed, with

furniture of dark moreen. the dressing-table of black oak be

crowd with an assortment of toilet requirement and appurtenance,

silver-mount and of most elegant workmanship.



then his wandering glance be arrest by something--a garment of

snowy whiteness--which have be lay over the back of a chair. Mr.

Nevill, cross to it, take it up gingerly and open it. it prove

to be a fine lawn _chemise de nuit_, frille and trim with

beautiful lace--a garment such as a duchess might have wear, but

certainly never intend to be wear by one of the opposite sex.



our young friend drop it as if it be a red-hot cinder, and,

sink into the near chair, cover his face with his hand. from

head to foot he feel as if he be one huge blush.









CHAPTER IV.

the squire of STANBROOK.



before proceed to narrate the sequel of the strange adventure of

the _soi-disant_ Mr. Frank Nevill, it may be as well that the reader

should be make acquainted with the circumstance to which be owe

his appearance on the King's highway in the character of an amateur

Claude Duval.



at the time with which our narrative have to do, Mr. Ambrose Cortelyon,

commonly know as Squire Cortelyon, of Stanbrook, an old family seat

in one of the most northern county of England, be well over his

seventieth birthday. throw by his horse more than twenty year

before, he have not only break his leg, but three or four of his rib

into the bargain.



surgical science in those day, especially in country place, be not

what it be now. his leg be badly set, with the result that from that

time he have be a partial cripple, who when he walk any distance

alone, have to do so with the help of a couple of stout stick, but who

usually prefer the arm of his factotum, Andry Luce, and one stick.



Andry--of whom we shall hear more later--be a man of forty, with a

big, shaggy head and the torso of an athlete set on the short, bow

leg of a dwarf. far, he be dumb (the result of a fright when a

child), a deficiency which only cause his employer to value he the

more. he be clever with his pen and at figure, and keep the Squire's

account and write most of his letter, for Mr. Cortelyon hate pen

work, and besides suffer occasionally from gout in his finger.



finally, Andry fill up his spare time by dabble in chemistry in an

amateurish fashion, be quite content to experiment on the

discovery of other, and have no ambition to adventure on any of

his own.



a full-length oil painting of Squire Cortelyon, take a short time

before his accident, and still in existence, represent he as a thin,

wiry-look man of medium height, close shaven, with a long, narrow

face--a handsome face, with its regular, clear-cut feature, most

people would call it; cold, unsympathetic light-blue eye, and a dry,

caustic smile. his dark, unpowdered hair, cut short in front, be

doubtless gather into a queue, only, as he stand face the

spectator, the picture fail to show it. he be dress in a

high-collared, swallow-tail, chocolate-color coat with gilt

button. his waistcoat be of white satin, elaborately embroider with

spray of flower. his small-clothe, tight-fitting and of some dark

weave material, reach to the ankle, where they be tie with a knot of

ribbon and be supplement by white silk stocking and buckled shoe.

round his throat be wound a soft cravat of many fold; his shirt be

frille, and he wear lace ruffle at his wrist. he stand in an easy

and not ungraceful posture, look right into the spectator's eye.

in one hand he clasp his snuff-box, deprive of which life for he

would have lose half its value.



although Squire Cortelyon court and love a cheap popularity, at

heart he be a man of a hard and gripe disposition, whose chief

object in life, more especially of late year, have be the

accumulation of wealth in the shape of landed property. even in early

life he have never either hunt or shoot, but, for all that, he

subscribe liberally to the near pack of hound, as also--but less

liberally--to the usual local charity. although he employ a couple

of keeper, he do not preserve too strictly, a fact which tend to

his popularity among his poor neighbor, while have an opposite

effect among those of his own standing in the county. in point of

fact, three-fourth of the game on his estate be shoot by his keeper

and send, under his direction, for sale to the near large town.



when Ambrose Cortelyon, at the age of thirty-five, come into his

patrimony, it be not only grievously burden with debt, but, as far

as mere acreage be concern, owe to extravagant living on the part

of his two immediate progenitor, have dwindle to little more than a

third of what it have be sixty year before. from the first the new

squire make up his mind that the folly of his father and grandfather

should not be repeat in his case. from the first he set two object

definitely before he, and never allow himself to lose sight of

they. object number one be to wipe off the burden of debt he have

inherit from his father. this, by the practice of rigid economy, he

be enable to do in the course of eight or ten year, after which he

begin to save. object number two be to become, in the course of time,

a large landowner, even as his great-grandfather and his more remote

ancestor right away back to the sixteenth century have be.



thus, in the course of time it come to pass that Ambrose Cortelyon

have become the owner of sundry considerable property (not all of

they situate in his own county, but none of they far off than a

day's ride) which, owe to one cause or another, have come into the

market. every season--and what be true then seem equally true

to-day--bring its own little crop of land proprietor who, owe

to improvidence or misfortune or both, have fall upon evil day, and

whenever there be a likely property in the neighborhood to be have a

bargain, the squire, or his agent Mr. Piljoy, be always to the fore.



with the former it be an article of faith that, for one reason or

other, land property would rise greatly in value in the course of

the next generation or two, and so constitute a stable inheritance for

those to come after he. in so believe the prescience with which he

credit himself be undoubtedly at fault. many thing be to happen

during the next half-century of which not even the most far-seeing of

the statesman of those day have the slight prevision.



Squire Cortelyon be turn forty before he marry. he fix his

mature affection on a banker's daughter, who bring he a dowry of

ten thousand pound, with the prospect of thirty thousand to follow at

her father's demise. but three year later the bank in which Mr.

Lowthian be senior partner fail, and the prospective thirty

thousand go in the general smash. such a loss to such a man be

undoubtedly a terrible blow. a couple of year later still his wife

die, leave he with one child,--a son. he have feel no particular

affection for she while live, and he be not hypocrite enough to

pretend to mourn she very deeply now she be dead.



Ambrose Cortelyon be one of those man who never feel comfortable, or

at home, in the presence of child, and as soon as Master Dick be

old enough he be pack off to a public school, and for the next

dozen or more year, except at holiday time, it be but little he see

either of his father or his home. from school he go to college, but

with his twenty-first birthday his career at Cambridge come to an end.

the life his father intend he for be that of a country gentleman,

with, perhaps, an M.P.-ship _in future_. where, then, would have be

the use of waste more time in compete for a degree which, even if

he should succeed in take it, would be of no after-value to he? far

well that he should spend a season or two in town, perfect

himself in his French meanwhile--the country swarm with _emigrés_

glad to give lesson for the mere pittance--and after that devote a

couple of year to the Grand Tour. Mr. Cortelyon would have his son a

man of the world, and neither a milksop nor a puritan. with his own

hand he put a copy of "Lord Chesterfield's Letters to his Son" into

Dick's valise. "a book to profit by," he say. "let I adjure you to

read and re-read it."



Dick feel more respect--which till he be grow-up have not be

unmixe with awe--than affection for his father. all his life Mr.

Cortelyon have be a reserved and undemonstrative man, and averse from

any display of feeling or sentiment. still, that his son be far

dearer to he than aught else in life, and that he look with secret

pride and hope to mould he in accordance with his own view and

wish, can hardly be doubt. the mistake he make be in imagine

that Dick be fashion on the same line, mental and moral, as

himself; whereas the lad take after his mother in almost every

particular. easy-go, affable to all, lead far more by his heart than

his head, everybody's friend and nobody's enemy but his own--how be

such a young man, with his handsome person, well-line purse, and a

certain element of rustic simplicity which still cling to he, to

escape shipwreck in the great maelstrom of London in one form or

another?



at any rate, Dick Cortelyon do not escape shipwreck in so far as the

utter ruin of his worldly prospect be concern. he have not be a

year in town before he commit the unpardonable folly--unpardonable

in the only son of Squire Cortelyon--of marry a fascinating little

actress of no particular ability, who at that time be play

"chambermaid" part at one of the patent theatre for a remuneration

of a guinea a week.



the marriage be keep by Dick a profound secret both from his father

and his friend. but it have to be tell the former when, some month

later, he summon Dick home on purpose to inform he that it be his

wish--really tantamount to a command on the part of such a man--that

he should "make up" to Miss Onoria Flood, the only daughter of a

neighbor, and do his good to secure she before any other suitor

appear on the scene.



when the fatal news be break to the squire he bundle Master Dick

out of door without a moment's hesitation. there and then he take an

oath that he would never forgive he, nor ever set eye on he again,

and he be a man who pride himself on keep his word. at once he

stop Dick's allowance.



some few year before these thing come to pass, the Squire's

grand-niece--granddaughter of his sister Agatha--an orphan leave

without mean beyond a narrow pittance of eighty pound a year, have

come to live at Stanbrook, no other home be open to she. although

there be a difference of some six year in their age, and although

they have only meet at interval, they have be to each other like eld

brother and young sister. from the first Miss Baynard have conceive

an almost passionate liking and admiration for her handsome,

kind-hearted kinsman, and now that poor Dick be leave home never to

return, she contrive to have a steal interview with he before he

go. although only just turn sixteen, she be in many thing wise

beyond her year, and before part from Dick she obtain from he

not be sure what his future movement might be, he give she the

address of his wife's uncle, who keep a tobacconist's shop in a street

off Holborn. that do, Dick kiss she and go, and with his go

half the sunshine seem to vanish out of Nell's life.



at once Dick Cortelyon break with his old life and all its

association. the fashionable world know he no more: he disappear,

he go under. he take a couple of furnish room in an obscure

neighborhood, and for the next few month his wife's earning and the

proceed of the sale of his watch and trinket keep the pair of they.

but there come a time when his wife could earn no more; and then a son

be bear to he. in this contingency he deem himself a fortunate man

in be able to get a lot of copying to do for a law firm in Chancery

Lane.



but poor Dick's trial and trouble--the fruit, as every reasonable

person must admit, of his own headstrong folly--be not destine to

be of long duration. when his child be about six month old he catch

a fever, and die after a very short illness. one of his last request

be that when all be over his wife should write and inform Miss

Baynard of his death. this Mrs. Cortelyon do not fail to do. her

letter convey the double news of Dick's death and the birth of his

son.



[illustration: "he give she the address of his wife's uncle."]



Miss Baynard at once take the letter to her uncle. his sallow face

become still sallower as he read the account of his son's death, but a

frown deeply than the girl have ever see on they before darken his

feature by the time he have come to the end of the letter.



"have Dick not be idiot enough to wed that play-act huzzy," he

say, "the lad would have be alive to-day. I owe his loss to she.

neither she nor her brat will I ever countenance or acknowledge. tell

she so from I. stay, though; you may send she this ten-pound note,

with the assurance that it be the last money she will ever receive at

my hand."



a few day later the note be return to the squire through the post,

accompany by a few unsigned line to the effect that the widow of

Richard Cortelyon would accept no help at the hand of the man who have

treat her husband with such inhuman cruelty.



not long after this Miss Baynard write to the widow, to the

address furnish by she in her letter, mention how attached she

have be to Dick, and hint delicately at the happiness it would

afford she to send Mrs. Cortelyon a little monetary help now and

again. but at the end of a fortnight her letter come back marked,

"gone away--present address not know," and enclose in an official

envelope. it have be open and reseal by the post-office

authority. as it happen, the letter fall into the squire's hand,

who, notice only the official envelope, open it without perceive

that it be address to his niece. as a consequence he at once send

for she.



after explain how it happen that he have open the letter, he

continue: "I be astonished and annoyed, Nell--very seriously

annoyed--that, after what thou hear I say two or three week ago,

thou should have choose of thy own accord to communicate with this

play-act creature, and even to offer to help she out of thy own

scanty mean. fortunately, the woman have disappear. no doubt she have

go back to the life and the companion that be most congenial to

she--curse on she for a vile baggage! to she I owe it that my boy

lie moulder in the grave. never again, Nell, on pain of offend

I past forgiveness, do thou attempt to have aught to do with she.

'tis beneath thee to notice such creature in any way--and she above

all other."



it be an injunction which Nell--who have listen to his tirade with a

sort of proud disdain and without a word of reply--determined to obey

or disobey as circumstance might determine. for the present she be

helpless to do more than she have do. unfortunately, she have mislay

the address give she by Dick at parting, otherwise she might perhaps

have be able to obtain tiding of Mrs. Cortelyon through the

latter's uncle, the London tobacconist.









CHAPTER V.

FAMILY matter.





four year pass away without bring any further tiding of the

widow and her child, during all which time their name be not once

mention between uncle and niece. by the latter their existence be

by no mean forget; she often think about they, often long to

see they. whether it ever enter the mind of Squire Cortelyon that he

have a live grandson be know to himself alone. he grow old and make

no sign.



meanwhile Miss Baynard have shoot up from a lanky slip of a girl into a

very beautiful young woman.



when she first go to live at Stanbrook, the squire, have no female

element in his house of a high status than that of housekeeper,

engage the service of Mrs. Budd--widow of the Rev. Onesimus

Budd--for the dual position of _gouvernante_ and companion to his

orphan niece. Mrs. Budd's duty as governess have long ago come to an

end, but therewith she have assume what to many people would have

seem the much more responsible and onerous post of chaperon. but,

although a clever little woman in her way, Mrs. Budd be nothing if

not easy-go. for she the wheel of existence be always well

oil. nothing disturb she much. responsibility slide off her like

water off a duck's back. life for she mean little more than a

sufficiency of sofa fit with the soft cushion. she be

excessively good-nature, and, hate to be worry herself, be

careful never to worry other. she and her charge get on capitally

together, chiefly because she be too wise ever to offer any very

strenuous opposition to the whim and vagary of that self-will

young woman. a mild protest, by way of easement to her conscience, she

do now and then venture upon, which, however, Miss Baynard would

brush aside with as little effort or compunction as she would a

cobweb.



to some of Squire Cortelyon's neighbor it seem an inconsistency on

his part that he, who have pack off his son to school at the early

possible age, should have take to his hearth, and have keep she

there, an orphan niece of no fortune, when he might so easily have rid

himself of she in the same way that he have rid himself of Dick. and

certainly, as have be remark, the squire be no lover of child,

and be generally credit with not have an ounce of sentiment in

CHAPTER VI.

a man who never forgive.

CHAPTER VII.

who shall be HEIR?





from that day forward Ambrose Cortelyon seem to regard his niece

the Squire be a man who expect those of his household, and all who

Squire become too weak to go out of door, even for a drive, and then

at Dr. Banks's request, that well-know physician, Dr. Mills, of

Lanchester, be call into consultation, but all he could do, after

make one or two minor suggestion, be to accord his full approval

to the treatment already adopt by his colleague.



"I willn't pay you your fee, doctor--hang I if I will, sir--till you

tell I what you think of I," say the Squire in his masterful way

when Dr. Mills be ready to go.



"well, Squire, to be frank with you, I think your condition a somewhat

grave one. but while there's life there's hope, you know. yes, yes, we

mustn't give up hope on any account; and you could not be in well

hand than those of my friend Dr. Banks."



"you would advise I to make my will, eh?" the cunning smile with

which he leer up into the physician's face hide a terrible anxiety at

the back of it.



the doctor purse out his lip. "in such matter it be always

advisable to be prepare, to take time by the forelock, as

one may say. and in your case, Mr. Cortelyon, I be inclined to

think--um--well, yes, that any testamentary arrangement you may have

to make should not----"



"I understand," break in the squire with a wave of his hand. "not a

word more be need. here be your fee. I be oblige to you for your

frankness; and so good-day to ye." he feel as if sentence of death have

just be pronounce on he.



yes, it be no long possible to cheat himself with vain hope of

could no long be ignore, and the soon he clasp it to he and

private information from Piljoy that a certain property, on which for

year he have set long eye, would be in the market before another

twelvemonth be over, and yet he, Ambrose Cortelyon, would not be

there to bid for it! again he ask himself what Providence be about.



still, however much he might rail and rebel in secret at the dark

prospect before he, know all the while how childish and futile it

be to do so, his hard face in nowise soften to those about he, and

he betray no slackness of interest in any of the little everyday

affair that go on around he.



but another spectre, besides that grisly one which Dr. Mills's word

have call up, begin to haunt he, hover round his pillow by night,

and never be far from his elbow between daybreak and dark. there

be only one way of exorcise it, as he know full well, and that be

by make his will. the entail have be cut off in his grandfather's

it be one which could not with safety be much long delay, unless

single living relative. whom, then, should he make his heir? for he

it be fast become the question of question.



oh, it be hard, hard, while he be still in what, rightly consider,

ought to be look upon as the prime of life, to have to part from the

and painful scraping to accumulate! but there be no help for it;

forgave.



it be just about this time that the Hon. Mrs. Bullivant, having hear

Mrs. Bullivant, when know to the world as Miss Onoria Flood, the only

but we know what happen when Dick be tell his father's goodwill and

event.



the Hon. Hector be a notorious gambler and _roué_, and within a

Mrs. Bullivant at this period of her life be what be generally

understand by the term "a fine woman," that be to say, she be build

as already state, the interview between she and the Squire last

to become a great landed proprietor, or rather, that her son should

have the fate prove propitious, Onoria would have be his

why should he not leave she a life-interest in his land property,

the same, at her decease, to devolve upon her son, on condition of his

not to be hastily decide upon. he would think it over. and he do.









CHAPTER VIII.

"a woman of a thousand."





about this time Tatham, the Squire's body-servant, fall ill, and at

his own request be allow to leave Stanbrook for awhile and go to

he and Miss Baynard have share the duty of the sick-room between

must be find to fill Tatham's place during his unavoidable absence.



now in the village--a populous and thriving one--the outlying house

but be at the time of her husband's death scarcely beyond middle

age, and of an active disposition, she presently begin to cast about

for some way not merely of add to her limited income, but of

banish from her life the idleness which her soul abhor.



be determined to find work, she take the first chance that come in

"the quality," and whenever none of the quality stand in need of her

nothing, till they should send for she.



when a message from Stanbrook one day reach she, she respond to it

with alacrity.



to the squire it seem very inconsiderate on Tatham's part that he

no help for it, it matter not a jot to he, he say, whom they

supply he with by way of temporary substitute. so, at the express

instance of Dr. Banks, Mrs. Dinkel be send for.



she be a woman of few word and strong nerve, who seem never to

a few day later, and Mr. Cortelyon have finally make up his mind,

bitter as the need for do so be to he. but it be indeed high

any rate, hinder I from remember you in my prayer."





[illustration: "his mother now produce the Squire's watch and

appendage."]





a cynical smile light up the Squire's haggard face. perhaps the

that it have be there only a few minute before he be fully

"I cannot imagine what make he do such a thing," she say, with tear

but the squire be snigger. "I trust, Onoria, you will do no such

it be in the course of the afternoon of the second day after Mrs.

Mr. Piljoy leave Stanbrook immediately after breakfast, and without

CHAPTER IX.

CONVERGING THREADS.





in the course of the same forenoon Andry Luce seek Miss Baynard with

the view of make a certain private communication to she. talk on

Andry's part be, of course, do by mean of his finger, but long

practice have make Nell an adept at read the language of the dumb.



Andry, who retain no recollection of his parent, in his brooding,

self-contain fashion have never really care but for two people,

to-wit, his young master, Dick Cortelyon, and Miss Baynard. one of

they be dead, and to the other be now give a double measure of that

love and devotion which have spring full-grown from his heart the

moment he first set eye on she, and have burn there with a steady,

unflickering flame ever since. she be the secret goddess at whose

shrine he worship daily. his love be unmixe with any taint of

ordinary passion, and be as absolutely pure as that of a father for

his child. it be the one well-spring of live water his maimed life

know. there be nothing in the wide world he would not have do, or

have attempt, at Miss Baynard's bidding.



his object in see she this morning be to enlighten she with regard

she be render terribly indignant, as Andry know full well she would

but what could be do? how could the purpose of this most iniquitous

in the matter as a new-bear babe. tear hot and passionate be shed

tear? they fall, only to be dry up as quickly as a summer shower.



now, it so happen that about this time a certain peripatetic dealer

in rare book, coin, and curio of different kind, of whom Mr.

Cortelyon have make sundry purchase at various time, call at

it be not so much that he have fall in love with it on his own

which his friend Mr. Delafosse, who be also a bibliophile and a

Ambrose Cortelyon, have long covet. although he believe himself to

be so near his end, it seem to he that he should die happy with

the precious stater shut up in his palm, and the knowledge that at

last it be his own.



yes, Nell should go to Dene House, take the MS. with she and

give she a hearty welcome--and be back home before noon on the morrow.

and instruction have perforce to be limit to a few whisper

he want do, and he be satisfied. she would set out in the course

of the afternoon, and be back by midday on the morrow.



about an hour later Dr. Banks arrive, accompany by Dr. Mills. the

Squire have express a wish to see the latter about once a week, and

although all the doctor in the world could have do nothing for he,

that be no reason why his whim should not be humor. but there be

no need for a lengthened visit, and the pair have come and go in the

course of half an hour.



this be the day fix for the return of Mr. Piljoy with the will, and

about half-past two a post-chaise drive up, from which, however, there

alight not the lawyer himself, but his manage clerk, Mr. Tew. Mr.

Piljoy, he report, be lay up with gout, and unable to come, but

he, Mr. Tew, be just as competent to see to the proper signing of the

will.



Mr. Tew be not sorry to be tell that the squire be asleep and must

on no account be disturb. he be both tired and hungry, and be glad

to be able to put the hospitality of Stanbrook to the proof before

have to attend to the business which have take he there.



it be not till close upon five o'clock that he be summon to the

but Andry Luce have be instruct to keep within hearing of his

Mr. Tew, having explain the cause of his employer's absence, go

deliberately, the testator give a nod of approval at the end of each

clause. five or six minute bring the reading to an end, and as he

witness be in readiness?"



the Squire nod. "ask Andry to summon the gardener and the groom.

they have be tell to hold themselves in readiness," he whisper.



the two subordinate in question be not long in make their

appearance, and very self-important, albeit somewhat sheepish, they

most of the old servant be legatee under the will, whereas they

be not mention in it. besides, it have be ascertain that they

could actually sign their name, which, for person of their class,

be regard in those day as a very considerable accomplishment.



then Andry, who have charge of the proceeding, bring in the Squire's

their employer's will, and that be enough for they.



Mr. Tew be not allow to leave Stanbrook till after dinner, nor,

Mrs. Budd and he dine alone. he be tell that Miss Baynard, to whom

he have be introduce early in the afternoon, have in the meantime

expect back till next day.



shortly after seven o'clock, Mr. Tew, who be begin to be a little

muddle with the quantity of old port he have imbibe, bade Mrs. Budd

an almost affectionate farewell (she be a widow, and, to his

thinking, still a charming woman), climb into his chaise, and be

drive off on his return to Arkrigg.



Night settle down over the old house. in those remote country part

people keep early hour, and when the hall clock chime the half-hour

past ten the only light leave burn in the Hall be the one in the

sick man's room. near it sit Nurse Dinkel busily knit--for she

could not bear her finger to be idle--but watchful and alert, as she

always be. the squire do not like to be look at as he lie there,

and from where she sit she could not see he for the heavy curtain

that shroud the head of the bed, but the slight movement of his

finger on the counterpane draw she to his side.



she be a woman of some education, and have a low and pleasant voice,

and as Mr. Cortelyon's night be often restless and wakeful, he have

get into the way of occasionally ask she to read aloud to he. she

do so take he for a time out of the dungeon of his own thought

and sometimes bring in its train the sleep he long for.



so to-night, after lie awake for some time, as motionless as if he

be already dead, he say, "Nurse!"



"yes, sir?"



"I want you to read to I." his voice be still very feeble, but

strong than it have be in the afternoon; such fluctuation be

frequent with he.



"yes, sir. what would you like? shall I go on with Mr. Pope from where

we leave off the night before last?"



"Aye, you cann't improve on he. draw back this curtain that I may the

well hear you."



when the curtain have be draw back Nurse Dinkel do not return to

her chair, but stand there, look at her patient, nurse an elbow

in either hand.



"Mr. Cortelyon, sir," she say after a brief pause, "I have something

on my mind which I wish most particularly to say to you, if you will

kindly give I leave to do so."



"surely, Mrs. Dinkel, I will listen to anything you may have to say.

but don't stand there while you talk. go back to your chair."



"thank you, sir," she say, as she resume her seat. "I will try not

to tire you, although what I wish to say may at the beginning seem a

bit tedious. you may or may not be aware, sir, that I have a son,

Cornelius by name, who be now turn thirty year of age. when he be

quite a boy--and a clever boy he be, though 'tis I who say it--the

late Sir Willoughby Freke take a great fancy to he. in the course of

time he go to London, at Sir Willoughby's expense, for he be bent

on study to become a doctor. and study he do to such good purpose

that he pass all his examination with fly color. hardly,

however, have he obtain his diploma before a very good offer be make

abroad any more. his home will be in London, but before settle down

there he have come to spend a little time with his old mother, from

whom he have be so long part. and now, sir, I come to the reason

why I have take on myself to trouble you with all these dry

"my son have bring a wonderful discovery back with he from the East.

accord to his account, it will cure certain disease after all

other medicine have be try in vain, and, in some case, will

almost bring dead people back to life. what the drug consist of I

cannot tell you, because that be my son's secret, and one which he

would not think of open his lip about even to I. all I know be

that the chief ingredient be the powdered bark of a certain tree, of

which he have bring a considerable supply back with he. Cornelius

feel as sure as it be possible for a man to be of anything that he

have only to introduce his discovery to the medical world of London to

find himself on the high road to a big fortune. his heart be buoy up

not merely by hope but by certainty.



"well, sir, no long ago than last Sunday afternoon, when you and

Miss Baynard be good enough to spare I from my duty for a few

hour, I have a long talk with my son, and take the liberty of tell

he about your illness. and what do you think he say, sir? why this:

'if Mr. Cortelyon could only be persuade into try my drug, I feel

sure that it would give he a new lease of life.' those be his very

word, sir--'a new lease of life.'"



the Squire lie silent for a little while. then he say, "and it be

your opinion that I ought to allow myself to be experiment upon by

this vaunted remedy of your son?"



"most emphatically it be, sir. Cornelius be no idle boaster; he always

know what he be talk about, and he would not have say what he do

without good reason. he try the drug again and again in several

desperate case before he leave Batavia, and in no instance be it a

failure."



"but I be an old man, Mrs. Dinkel, and my case be not one of any

particular disorder, but a gradual decay of the vital force, which

can have but one end--and that be now close at hand."



"don't say that, sir, I beg. who can say what wonder my son's remedy

might not effect even in your case, as it have already do in those of

other? it be true that neither Dr. Banks nor Dr. Mills seem able to

do anything for you, but that be no reason why you should refuse the

help now offer you from another source. my son know your age within

a year or two; I describe to he all about your illness, and yet for

all that, it be his deliberate opinion that he can give you a fresh

lease of life."



again the Squire lie for some time without speak. "only one quack

the more," he murmur to himself with a touch of his old cynicism.

"well, why not? from the high to the low they're quack, every

mother's son of 'em. as it be, I'm at death's door already, and if the

fellow can do I no good, I'll defy he to do I much harm."



then he say aloud: "D'you know, I'm half inclined to let this son of

yours experiment upon I, if only to take some of the brag out of he

and prove to he that in such a case as mine his wonder-work stuff

be no more effectual than a dose of senna would be."



"then you _will_ try it, sir! that be all I ask. in any case, no harm

can come of it."



"my own opinion exactly"--with a dismal attempt at a chuckle. "yes, I

agree to try it. only, the affair must be keep secret; outside this

room nobody must know about it, unless it be my man, Andry Luce. and

now, when can this son of yours be smuggle into the house?"



"it's only a little past eleven o'clock, sir, and if you think you can

spare I, I will go at once and bring he back with I. the servant

be all abe, and my son could come and go without one of they be a

whit the wiser."



"that's a very good notion of your, damme! go at once, my dear woman;

but first give I a drink of that cordial. I shall want nothing till

you get back. and if I can coax that shy dog, Morpheus, to keep I

company meanwhile, so much the well."









CHAPTER X.

the SEQUEL of MISS BAYNARD's adventure.





it be with a bitter sense of helplessness that Miss Baynard continue

with the exception of a certain legacy to herself and sundry small

bequest to old servant, the whole of her uncle's wealth, both in

land and money, would go to Mrs. Bullivant and her son, who be not

even cousin six time remove, cut she to the quick. the amazing

injustice of the thing, so to speak, strike she dumb. to think that a

man who know full well his span of life have dwindle to a few brief

hour should, in cold blood, choose to perpetrate so black a sin--for

in her eye it be nothing less--be to nell wholly inconceivable. and

all for what? merely because his son have marry beneath he, and have

thereby bring to naught a certain ambitious scheme on which his

heart have be set. and now the innocent child be to suffer for its

father's fault, if fault it be. oh, it be monstrous--monstrous!



of one thing she be quite sure: she would never touch her uncle's

legacy. every shilling of it should go to the boy. but what be such a

pittance in comparison with the income which, when he should come of

age, ought to be his of inalienable right? yet his name be not once

mention in the will! the last of the Cortelyons--bone of his bone,

and flesh of his flesh--might die in a gutter or come to the gallow

for anything the old man care. such a revenge be more than human; it

be fiendish, and could only have be prompt by the devil. Nell

burn from head to foot with a fine flame of indignation when she

think of these thing, and for the next forty-eight hour she could

think of nothing else.



it be in the course of the second afternoon after Andry Luce have tell

she that she happen--herself unseen--to overhear the two doctor

outside," one of they say. to which the other reply: "Hum! I

daresay you be right. but I shouldn't be a bit surprised if he be

to go off in his sleep between now and to-morrow."



Nell give one quick gasp, and a shudder run through she from head to

but to hear from the lip of those who know that the end be so close

if she have suddenly come face to face with a skeleton.



it be the day Mr. Piljoy have promise to bring the will for the

purpose of have it sign, and as she remember this she could not

help say to herself: "if I could only get hold of it and destroy

it, my uncle would hardly live to sign another in its stead, and Evan,

as his grandfather's heir-at-law, would succeed to everything!"



then a little derisive laugh at her folly break from her lip. get

hold of the will, forsooth! why, she would not be allow to so much

about an hour later her uncle send for she. it be in connection with

know that.



about two o'clock Mr. Tew, in Mr. Piljoy's stead, arrive with the

Nell's heart grow cold as she listen. why do not providence

all would have be well. as it be, she could but wring her hand in

she be go sadly upstairs to her own room (after see Mr. Tew

conscious lead up to it on her own part, be nothing less than, in

when a young spark of nineteen or twenty, Dick Cortelyon, on the

occasion of one of his brief visit at home, have attend a fancy ball

act through some sub-conscious channel, which, while ask herself

we know at what decision she arrive. instead of scout the idea and

cast it from she, as ninety-nine young woman out of every hundred

practical issue. whatever risk might be connect with, or follow on,

the affair she be prepared to face, if only she could thereby insure

the destruction of her uncle's iniquitous will.



fortunately for she, when she come to consider, several thing seem

in the first place, everything in the way of dress and accessory

her errand to Mr. Delafosse.



for the last time she ask herself, "shall I adventure it, or shall I

she make it her first business to see John Dyce and have a little

the change be affect in due course, and a very handsome and dashing

if they have be make for she.



but servant have pry eye, and not thus would it do for she to be

see leave the house; besides, there be the risk of encounter

to wit, a long riding-skirt, and a mantle with a hood to it, the

for lady travel on horseback.



into a couple of saddle-bag, which John Dyce have supply she with,

she have already stow away a number of thing. then, when all be

ready, she go down by way of the back staircase, and so out of the

horse, and two minute later they be canter down the avenue.



they ride through the village, and so on their way for a couple of

and hood mantle (her hair having be previously brush back from

John, meanwhile, have be change Peggy's sidesaddle for an ordinary

that she be a fearless horsewoman. whatever awkwardness or
