but presently she have other thing to think of than any little hot and

cold shiver of her own. in the holster in front of she be stick a

brace of unloaded pistol. John's pistol, however, be fully

charge.





how Miss Baynard speed on her hare-brain expedition have already be

tell: how she mistook the chaise of a stranger for that of Mr. Tew;

how she be fire at, but escape with nothing bad than a fright;

and how the notorious Captain Nightshade appear in the nick of time

and act as her guide as far as Rockmount, where, under the name of

Mr. Frank Nevill (that of a cousin in India) she be make welcome, and

find shelter for the night.



we leave she just after Mr. Cope-Ellerslie's housekeeper have show she

to her chamber; and now that the two thread of our narrative have

be bring together we will take up her history from the follow

morning.



when "Mr. Frank Nevill" go downstairs he find an excellent

breakfast await he in the same room into which he have be show

overnight. he be wait upon by Mrs. Dobson, who express much

concern at the smallness of his appetite. when the meal have come to an

end she say, "at what o'clock, sir, would you like your horse to be

bring round?"



"as soon as it can be get ready, for I be anxious to get on my way."



in ten more minute he be in the saddle. in accordance with Mr.

Ellerslie's promise, a serve-man on horseback be in readiness to

show he the way as far as the Whinbarrow road. he do not part from

Mrs. Dobson without ask she to convey to her master his warm

thank for the hospitality which have be extend to he; nor do he

take it.



as he turn away from the house he give it a long backward look. it

be a two-storied domicile, plain to the verge of ugliness, build of

roughly-hewn block of the dark gray stone of the country. its wall

be of great thickness, and it be roof with huge slab of slate,

well fit to withstand the fierce gale which assail it during the

stony, desolate moorland, which spread away on every side till it be

lose in the distance. no other homestead or sign of man's occupancy or

vicinage be anywhere visible. a narrow rut lane, originally, no

doubt, nothing more than a sheep track, pass close by it, seemingly

come from nowhere and lead to nowhere. Frank Nevill shudder as

he look. what must it be like, he ask himself, to live there in

winter? what man in his proper sense would think of build a house

on such a spot? and yet Mr. Cope-Ellerslie seem well satisfied to

live there!



after traverse the lane for a matter of three or four mile, Frank

and his conductor emerge on one of the great highway run due

north and south. cross this, they find themselves after a little

infallibly have lose himself. Frank's guide, however, evidently know

every foot of the way, and at the end of a couple of hour, at a point

where the cross-road they have be traverse debouch into one much

wide, he pull up his horse and say: "this be the Whinbarrow road,

sir; six mile straight ahead will bring you to Dunthale Prior. do you

wish I to go any far with you, sir?"



they be almost the first word the man have speak, and Frank, as in

honor bind, have refrain from put any question to he.



he now dismiss he with thank and a little present for himself.

twenty minute later he draw rein and dismount at the first

toll-bar, at which place it have be arrange that John Dyce should

await his arrival.



and there honest John be, and a glad man be he to set eye again on

his young mistress. never before have he pass so wretched a night.

fear and anxiety have render he half crazy, and have put all thought

of sleep out of his head.



as already state, the keeper of the toll-bar be a cousin of

John Dyce; and Mrs. Nixon, his wife, now proceed to show "Mr.

Nevill" into a neat little bedroom. it be the last time that young

gentleman be see by mortal eye. at the end of half-an-hour Miss

Baynard--stately and gracious, but with a defiant sparkle in her eye

which seem to say, "challenge I who dare!"--issued from the chamber

and make her way downstairs.





Miss Baynard reach Dene House on the stroke of noon, where she be

warmly welcome by Mr. Delafosse and his wife. the old bibliophile

prove to be quite willing to exchange his gold stater of Epaticcus

for the rare MS. on vellum which Nell have bring with she. although

genuinely grieve to receive such a bad account of his old friend, he

could not help remind himself that there be several rarity in

Cortelyon's collection the possession of which he have long envy he.

well, we must all die some time, and as his friend's collection would

be sure to come to the hammer, there would at length be a possibility

of his become the owner of such article as he especially covet.

all the more would they be value by he for having be the property

of a man he so highly esteemed.



after join the Dene House family over their three o'clock dinner,

Miss Baynard set out on her return, and, there be nothing this time

heart that she dismount.



she go up to her uncle's room without delay when tell that he have

more than once ask for she. "there's a great improvement in he

to-day, my dear," Mrs. Budd have say to she in the entrance hall. "Dr.

Banks be quite strike by the change when he call this morning."



Nell find her uncle awake. his eye meet hers questioningly as she

enter the room, but when she produce the coveted coin and place it

in his hand his face light up wonderfully. "good lass! good lass!"

he murmur. then he give a sigh of relief, and his lean finger

close lovingly over the stater.





as to whether Miss Baynard's attempt to purloin her uncle's will be,

or be not, under the circumstance morally justifiable, the writer

wish it to be understand that the point be one with which he

consider himself in nowise concern. his duty, as he apprehend it,

be simply that of a recorder of fact, without take on himself

either to justify or condemn any action, good, bad, or indifferent on

the part of his character, who be allow to go their own way

without let or hindrance, and as we all have to do, must accept and

make the good of whatever consequence may result therefrom.









CHAPTER XI.

"little short of MIRACULOUS."





yes, as Mrs. Budd have tell Miss Baynard, there be a decided change

for the well in Mr. Cortelyon's condition, but by what mean the

change in question have be bring about be know to three people

only--the sick man, his nurse, and the latter's son.



Cornelius Dinkel have go to Stanbrook in obedience to his mother's

midnight summon, take with he a small quantity of his remedy, and

have spend an hour with the squire, unknown to any of the household.



he be a tall, sallow, dry-up man, who look as if all the juice

of his body have be suck out of he by the heat of a tropical

climate. he be thirty year old, but might well have be take for a

man of forty-five. nobody would have venture to call he handsome,

but his expression be one of marked intelligence, in combination with

considerable will-power and great tenacity of purpose.



"Mr. Cortelyon, my son," say Mrs. Dinkel, as she introduce the young

doctor into the sick room.



Dinkel bow gravely. the Squire blink his eye; he would have

nod, but have not strength to do so.



then he say, speak in a thin whisper, break by frequent gasping

for breath: "your mother inform I that you have bring some

wonderful discovery back home with you, and she would fain cozen I

into the belief that by mean of it you can succeed in prolong the

life of a moribund like I. I tell you at once that I don't believe in

your ability to do anything of the kind. no, damme! I'm too far go

for any hanky-panky of that sort, and both Banks and Mills would

simply dub you a quack for your pain."



Dinkel's face remain impassive. "permit I, Mr. Cortelyon," he say,

and with that he proceed to submit the other to a brief but

search examination. till it have come to an end no one speak. then

take up a position on the hearth with his back to the fire, and

speak in the tone of one who feel himself master of the situation,

he break the silence:



"your case, Mr. Cortelyon, I find to be exactly such as my mother

describe it to I. that I can permanently cure you I at once admit to

be an impossibility. you be too advanced in life, and your

constitution be too nearly wear out, to warrant any such hope. but

that I can succeed in prolong your life for week, nay, it may well

be for several month to come, I make no manner of doubt--such be the

marvellous efficacy of the remedy I have bring back with I from

this, to a man who have every reason for believe that a few more

it be too marvellous to credit.



for a little while he be too overcome to speak. then he murmur, and

cannot believe it--I cannot!"



"nevertheless, Mr. Cortelyon, I be not deal in romance--heaven

eating. I have bring with I a small quantity of my remedy. will you

look like an amiable old woman.



"first of all, I should like to know what this so-call wonder-worker

of yours be compound of," he murmur, after a time.



Dinkel shrug his shoulder. "its chief constituent be the dried and

powder bark of the tatao tree--a tree indigenous to the island of

Java. the other ingredient consist of sundry drug in certain fix

proportion, the secret of which I be not at present prepare to

divulge."



"quite right--quite right. very sensible on your part. you don't want

to poison I, I suppose--hey?"



the ghost of a smile flit across Dinkel's rugged face. "what should

I gain by that, sir? only the hangman's noose. I think you ought to

credit I with a desire for lengthen your day, not for shorten

they."



"it would puzzle you to make 'em much short than they seem likely to

stuff of your, not because I've any faith in it, mind you, but merely

the wonderfully clever fellow you're inclined to crack yourself up as

be."



not for day have the squire speak so much in so short a time, and as

he could not have be in more competent hand, and before long he be

from his waistcoat pocket Dinkel extract a tiny phial, no big

"you willn't find it at all disagreeable," he say, as he proffer the

spoon and its content to the squire.



"it taste not unlike the liquorice-root I use to be fond of when a

lad," murmur the latter half a minute later, and with that his eye

Dinkel hold up his hand, and for a little space neither he nor his

go well, as I have every reason to think it will, he willn't awake for

five or six hour. I will go now, and return between six and seven

o'clock."



as already state, Dr. Banks, when he call as usual in the course of

improvement in his patient's condition, when, accord to all the

rule and regulation of medical science, he ought to have be

the pan--the sudden flare-up of a candle before it drop into

evidently underrate the strength of his constitution."



but next day, and the day after that, a still further improvement

there be nothing to be do and very little to be say, and the two

"to none at all, I give you my word. I be send he the same mixture

"possibly at you, bank, but certainly not at I," rejoin the other

"Humph! you seem to be bless with a very short memory, Mills. your

"well, well; we be all liable to err, of course. still, I'm afraid

miraculous."



Dr. Banks grunt. he be too indignant to reply in word. only to

himself he say, "I always set you down in my own mind as a humbug,

and now I'm more convinced of it than ever."



as the reader will have rightly surmise, the marvellous change in

Squire Cortelyon be wholly and solely due to the drug administer to

he by Cornelius Dinkel. already, as we have see, he be able to sit

up--although only for a short hour at first--and transact business;

little later he even get so far as, with the help of Andry Luce's arm,

to take short stroll about the ground.



but this eminently satisfactory state of thing could only be

Mrs. Dinkel remain at Stanbrook, nor, although he be so much

perhaps it seem to he that so long as he could succeed in retain

her service he would have a firm hold on those of her son. besides,

his man Tatham be not yet able to resume his duty.



so interested be young Dinkel in the case of Mr. Cortelyon that for

he be gift with the patience, slow but sure, of his father's race,

and be content to wait.



by this time it have got rumor about the country-side that the

of some magic compound he have bring with he from abroad. far,

it be commonly report that so long as Dinkel continue to practise

among other, the whisper go that the Squire have sell himself, body

these rumor be not lessen by Dinkel's mode of life. he have fit

up an old shed at the back of his mother's cottage, and there he

conduct his experiment. strange-color flame would often be see

issue from its chimney after dark, and one or two bold spirit,

who have venture to pry upon he, aver that they have see he

warm his hand at a big glass jar which give off blue spark when

he touch it. evidently he be a man to be both shun and fear.



but the love of life burn strongly in we. not merely be we desirous

of prolong our own existence, but the life of those dear to we,

and among the villager be three mother who, their child having

be give up by the local doctor, go to Cornelius Dinkel as a last

resource, and pray he with tear in their eye to try to save their

little one. he do try, and in two case out of the three he

still, the country people, with their ingrain superstitious

they mutter among themselves. if he could prolong "th' owd Squire's"

life, why couldn't he save Molly Grigg's child?--and why don't he

try his hand on old Tommy Binns, who be only eighty-seven when he

die?









CHAPTER XII.

a startling RECOGNITION.





in view of the astonishing and wholly unexpected change for the well

[illustration: "she have recognize the notorious Captain Nightshade."]

such a speech address nowadays by a young man to a young woman would

"and I on my part, Mr. Dare, can say in all sincerity that I be very

CHAPTER XIII.

love the CONQUEROR.





although Dare have not succeed in persuade Miss Baynard to reverse

dare go direct from Chelsea to Holborn. Miss Baynard have say that

find Mr. McManus as usual in his shop, Dare at once challenge he

and this to the man who have confess to be Captain Nightshade!

Dare have be quite right in his surmise as to the reason which have

she have only be a couple of day back at Stanbrook when a small

in some thing she shall, my dear Nell, but not in all, as you be

but who the sender of the mask be remain as much a mystery as

CHAPTER XIV.

a fresh actor on the scene.





except in a few occasional instance, Mr. Ambrose Cortelyon, who

pride himself on his possession of an unbiassed mind, be not in the

habit of be unreasonable either in his demand or his expectation,

whether they concern himself or other. thus, he be quite aware

under these circumstance, the Squire could bear to look forward to

Dinkel's proximate departure with tolerable equanimity.



Dr. Banks, at the Squire's request, still keep up his visit to the

not do well than keep on with the old medicine."



"of course we couldn't, bank," the Squire would respond with a

along as soon as you like."



then would bank take his departure, know well that not one

but he have a large family, and could not afford to quarrel with his

in another.



he have hear all that common report have to tell he about Dinkel, and

pour down the kitchen sink.



to himself he stigmatize Cornelius Dinkel as a "Son of the Devil."





but what about the Hon. Mrs. Bullivant all this time?

CHAPTER XV.

"FATE points the WAY."





"well, how do you fare? how much long be the old scoundrel go to

keep Beelzebub out of his own?"

"for all that, it be one which might be worth consider."

the day follow each other till a week have go by, and Captain

[illustration: "the body of the 'man-witch' have be find shoot

through the heart."]

by any one.



but it be not so much the fact of come unexpectedly on her brother

fall in the course of the day.



"great heaven! Wilton, what ail you? what have happen to you?"

cry Mrs. Bullivant.



"for God's sake not so loud! such an adventure!" he pant. "set upon

by two ruffian in a lonely part of the road. one of 'em I manage to

knock over with a lefthander--then take to my heel. if I haven't

they'd have bludgeon my brain out. two to one, you know."



"what a narrow escape for you! but what have become of your hat?"



"I've not lose it, have I?" he gasp, while a great terror leap into

his eye. "if so, I'm lose too!" a moment later his expression

smile. "I've get it all the while. it fall off while I be run and

as the rascal be not far behind I I make a dash at it and cram

it into one of my pocket. it will look a pretty object to-morrow,

_san doute_. but now to bed, for I'm dog-tired."



"shall I send you up some hot water and----"



"curse it all, no! I want no eye but your to see I to-night." he

glare at she for a moment as if he be about to strike she. then with

a shrug and a sudden dropping of his hand, he say. "forgive I,

Onny, I'm not myself to-night." and with that he pass she and go

swiftly upstairs, and presently she hear the key turn in the lock

of his room.



it be the recollection of this scene which shake she with such a

any truth in the story of his encounter with the two man? but, above

all, have he have any hand in last night's tragedy? that he be utterly

unscrupulous she have long know, and she divine, without know,

that in his nature there be dark unsounde depth in which the most

ghastly secret might be hide up forever. she be only too well aware

the way. to he it might, and most likely would, mean all the

she wait his come with a quake heart. she be sit in a mixed

light, that of the die afternoon and that throw out by the glowing

he stand there with the handle in his hand, without advance. "well,

very different from his usual smooth cultivate tone. "Dinkel's

dead--shoot through the heart last night, presumably when on his way

back from Stanbrook. body find early this morning by some hedger on

their way to work. what will happen now, I wonder? there's the rub,

both for you and I."



"I have already hear. the Tuxford carrier bring the news about an

hour ago."



"have I know that I needn't have hurry back, as I do, on purpose to

tell you. but no matter."



"have any trace of the--the perpetrator of the crime be discover,

or have they any idea where to look for he?"



"'pon my soul, I don't know. I never ask. 'twas a point that have no

interest for I. but now I'll go upstairs and make myself presentable,

and join you presently over a cup of tea. we have have a famous

scamper, the boy and I. but he will be with you in a minute or two."



after tea they play ecarté for a couple of hour, and never have Mrs.

Bullivant see her brother more cheerful and at his ease. she go to

bed not know what to think.









CHAPTER XVI.

the shear of ATROPOS.



the news of the tragedy in Threeways Spinny reach Stanbrook about

nine o'clock in the morning. the body of the murder man have already

be take home, and it be Mrs. Dinkel's next-door neighbor who be

depute to convey the sad tiding to she.



she be on the point of take the squire's breakfast upstairs when

the man arrive at the Hall, and ask to see she.



ten minute later Mrs. Dinkel enter her patient's room. like the

thoughtful creature she be, even in the midst of her distress she have

not forget the breakfast tray. having place it on the table by the

bedside, she turn to the squire, and, in a voice which not all her

effort could render firm, say:



"Sir, a great misfortune have befall I--the most terrible that could

have happen. my son have be murder! the tiding have just reach

I. his body be find early this morning in Threeways Spinny. he have

be shoot through the heart--he who have not an enemy in the world!

Sir, I must leave here at once. I be want at home, as you can well

conceive; but if----"



"dead! your son dead!" shriek the squire, almost as shrilly as a

woman might have do. then for a few second he remain speechless.

his heart stop beat, and a black veil drop before his eye.

but the very force of the shock bring its own reaction. he fling up

his arm, and then let they drop helplessly on the bed. "in that case,

what be to become of I?" he moan.



"the Lord in heaven only know, sir, for I'm sure I don't," answer

Mrs. Dinkel. it be all she could do to crush down her emotion.



the Squire sink back on his pillow with a groan. the bereaved mother

professional instinct in she, not like to leave he.



of a sudden he beckon she to go close to he, and when she have do

grayish purple, and a thin froth have gather at their corner. his

eye be the eye of a terror-hunt soul bring to bay, and yet

ready to turn and curse with its late breath the inexorable fate

which have drive it there.



"don't think I do not pity you, because that would be a mistake on

your part," he say. "I pity you and sympathize with you most

sincerely. but--but your son must have leave a lot of the drug--you

know what I mean--behind he. don't you think so, hey? and--and as

soon as ever you can spare time--in the course of the day, you

know--you will have a thorough search make, and ascertain the

quantity, and let I know at the early possible moment, willn't you?

yes, yes; he must have leave quite a considerable quantity ready

prepared. I feel sure of it; so don't forget to send I word as soon

as you can."



there be a terrible eagerness in the way he speak, and he would not

loose his hold of she till she have promise he, that he should hear

from she in the course of the forenoon.



when she be go her place be take by Miss Baynard.



that morning the Squire's breakfast be send away untasted, and he

make no effort to get up. anxiety hold he as with a vise--an anxiety

shoot through and through with foreboding the most dire. he lie

without speak, watch with feverish eye the slow-move finger

of the clock on the chimney-piece, each of whose solemn tick seem

to he to mark a stitch in the tapestry of Doom. it be a few minute

together with a letter, both of they address to "Ambrose Cortelyon,

Esq.," and both of they just bring by a special messenger. the sick

"open the letter and read it aloud, Nell," he say, as soon as the

servant have leave the room. it be not merely that he have lose the

control of his finger--he shake from head to foot like one in an ague

Nell do as she be bid.





"honored Sir" (she read), "in accordance with your wish and my own

promise, I have make diligent and careful search in every corner,

cupboard, and drawer of the room in which my poor son mix his

physics and attend to his doctoring business, with the result (and

it grieve I much to have to tell it you) that I have not succeed

in find more than two phial of the stuff ready mix for taking,

the which, under cover, I herewith send you.



"it would appear to have be my son's custom not to prepare any large

quantity of the drug beforehand, perhaps--but on this point I speak

without certainty--because he find that some portion of its virtue

be lose with keeping.



   "I remain, honor sir,



             "your obedient, humble servant,



                              "Martha Dinkel.



"P.S.--Since write the above I have make another thorough search,

high and low, in every nook and corner of the premise, but it have

prove a sheer waste of time.



"Mr. Cortelyon, sir, in the midst of my own distress, permit I

respectfully to observe that my heart bleed for you."





when Nell have read to the last word, the Squire make no comment aloud,

only to himself he murmur: "_Mors ultima linea rerum est_." he have

not open his Horace for year, but the line come back to he quite

power could save he. well, accord to all human calculation he

ought to have be dead and bury a number of week ago, but another

misfortune which no one could have foresee, it have come prematurely

to do be to wrap his toga about he and await the end with silent

stoicism.



although he take the two remain dose of the drug in due course, he

dead letter. he have do with it, and it have do with he. they be

quit.



so, day after day and night after night, he lie in the big four

and, between his fitful snatch of sleep, thinking, ever think,

strange and awesome thought must oftentime have be his as he lie

there in grim resolute silence, wait for his "order of release"!



his niece and Andry Luce take it in turn to watch by he. it be an

the Squire open his eye and favor they with one of his sardonic

"eh-eh! come to see the last of your handiwork?" he say, and already

I'm sure. and besides, my case be such an interesting and uncommon

and at the end you will know no more about it than you do now. yes,

yes, very kind and attentive of you; but as for your physic, I'll have

no more on't--that's flat. throw it to the dog, as Shakespeare say.

and now, 'I have an exposition of sleep come upon I.'"



of course they could do nothing, and to Miss Baynard they be candid

enough to admit as much. it be a sad state of thing.



and so the muffle hour steal after each other one by one till a week

no arrest have yet be make in connection with the murder of Cornelius

on a certain afternoon, somewhat to Miss Baynard's surprise, Mrs.

one day he bring back something which be more than a rumor. it be

little persuasion be need to induce Mrs. Bullivant to fall in with

More than once--indeed quite a number of time--when talk over she

on hear that Mrs. Bullivant have arrive, Miss Baynard go

"how be he, dear Miss Baynard?" be Mrs. Bullivant's first word. she

speak in hush tone, although as yet she have get no farth than the

"he be sink fast, and be almost speechless."



"you shock I more than I can say." and, to do her justice, for the

Aloud she go on: "I have not the faint idea that his illness have

when Mrs. Bullivant and Nell enter the sick room together some

Mr. Cortelyon lie with closed eye and with both arm extend on the

and so daylight slide slowly into dark, and the two woman still keep

Alas! it be already too late. he sink back, gasp for breath, with

Mrs. Bullivant dab her eye with her handkerchief. "poor dear! I be

so thankful he do not suffer much," she say. "that he should wander

a little in his mind at the last be not to be wonder at. nearly all

aged people do that when they be die."









CHAPTER XVII.

an ASTOUNDING DISCOVERY.





it be five day later.



the funeral be over. everything have be do decently and in order,

and in the great drawing-room at Stanbrook, the shutter of which of

late year have be rarely open, a small company be assemble, by

invitation of Mr. Piljoy, to hear the reading of the dead man's will.



Miss Baynard and Mrs. Budd sit together on one of the couch; a

little way remove, in stately isolation, sit Mrs. Bullivant; while

Mrs. Dace, the housekeeper, remain modestly in the background, with

Andry Luce and two or three other old servant to keep her company.



the gentleman comprise Mr. Herries, the vicar; Mr. Delafosse, Sir

James Dalrymple, of Langrig, and Squire Staniforth, of Claypool; the

two latter of whom, at Mr. Cortelyon's request, have agree to act as

trustee under his will. they be clear-head, thoroughly practical

man, with plenty of leisure on their hand, and, as such, have

recommend themselves to the late squire, who be their senior by

more than a score year, and have know their father before they.



Mr. Piljoy sit by himself at the big oval table in the centre of the

room. the will, as yet unopened, lie there in front of he.



when everybody have settle into their place and the door be finally

for the purpose of read to you, I may just remark for the

information of everybody, and in order to satisfy any curiosity which

might otherwise be feel on the point, that this be not the first

testament draw up by I for the late Mr. Cortelyon. there be a much

early will, the provision of which, I need scarcely tell you, be

of a widely different nature from those of the present one; but that

will be destroy at the time of the unhappy quarrel between father

and son, of which, I daresay, most of those here have some cognizance.

for the next few year no will of any kind be in existence, nor could

Mr. Cortelyon be persuade into make another till he find himself

overtake by illness of a very serious kind. I will now, with your

permission, proceed to open and read the will."



Sir James tap his snuff-box, open it, offer it to his

co-trustee, and then indulge himself with a large pinch. the servant

the will itself be enclose in a sheet of parchment secure with two

the envelope aside, he proceed to unfold and straighten out the

apparently, however, he have forget to how many page the document

extend, and in order to satisfy himself on the point, before

only five or six in all--till he come to the last one, on reach

which his eye instinctively travel to the foot of it.



next instant he give a start and spring to his foot, his eye still

glue to the bottom of the will. he look daze--thunderstruck--and

well he might.



"what be the meaning of this?" he cry. "what devilry have be at

work? the will be unsigned!"



and so, indeed, it prove to be. there be the space for the three

signature, those of the testator and the two witness, but the

over the scene that ensue we need not linger. the servant be tell

that they be no long want, and go back to their duty

"of course the will as it stand be wholly inoperative, still, Mr.

there be no need for you to read out the different clause; all we

want be to be make acquainted with the main feature of the document."

not trouble you. to the testator's old friend, Mr. Delafosse"--bowing

to that gentleman--"are bequeath sundry coin, medal, and other

curio, a list of which will be find among the private paper of the

deceased. then I must not omit to mention that to each of you

gentleman, for your trouble in act as trustee, be leave the sum of

one hundred and fifty guineas; and to Mr. Herries a similar sum for

distribution among the deserve poor of the parish. with regard to

his niece, Miss Baynard"--here he favor Nell with a bow--"the

testator's instruction be that a sum of money be invest in her

of three hundred pound, the which she shall enjoy for life, the

principal at her demise to be divide among certain specify

charitable institution. to the Hon. Mrs. Bullivant"--a bow for that

lady--"is leave the sum of three thousand pound in hard cash. every

thing else of which the testator may die possess--include the

border of Yorkshire, and certain other small estate--be willed in

condition that on come of age he add to his present cognoman that

he cease, and Sir James and his friend stare at each other in sheer

"both Sir James and I be under the impression that the late Mr.

Cortelyon have a grandson. his only son die some few year ago, do he

not, leave behind he a widow and one child?"



"he do."



"and yet in your summary of the content of the will you make no

mention of either of their name."



"the widow die some time ago. there be no mention of the boy's name

on my part for the very good reason that it be not include in the

will. Mr. Cortelyon never forgave his son's marriage, and refuse in

any way to acknowledge his grandchild."



again the two man look at each other, and again they refrain from

give expression to the thought at work within they.



then say Sir James: "an unsigned will be so much waste paper. in case

no other will, duly execute, should turn up, what will happen?"



"only one thing can happen. the case will resolve itself into one of

intestacy, and everything--lock, stock, and barrel--will go to the

heir-at-law, that be to say, to the grandson of whom mention have just

the two gentleman nod. that be their own view exactly.



Miss Baynard have sit all this time without speak or stirring. when

Nell as be dumfounde as Mrs. Bullivant by the turn event have take.

she have not clearly comprehend what the result would be of the will

she, and her heart swell with thankfulness.



but of a sudden, a shiver of apprehension run through she. Mr.

Staniforth be speak:



"have you any reason whatever, Mr. Piljoy, for suppose that any

other will than this unsigned one be in existence?"



not miss baynard only, but Mrs. Bullivant as well, await the

Mr. Piljoy shrug his shoulder. "that, sir, be a question which

just now I be hardly prepared to answer, and for this reason, that no

proceed to explain."



he lie back in the big library chair, clear his voice, and toy

with his spectacle for a few second before proceed.



"in accordance with Mr. Cortelyon's instruction, his will be draw

up by I in duplicate. this be do as a provision of safety; in the

event of any hitch or blunder occur in the signing or witnessing

word, 'Ambrose Cortelyon--his will' write across the face of it

that, through some mischance, the unsigned will got substitute for

the sign one in the envelope. if my supposition have any basis of

fact, the question that naturally follow be, what become of the

duplicate will? be there any one present, who be in a position to

throw any light on the point involve?"



whether consciously or unconsciously, as he ask the question his

follow those of Mr. Piljoy.



Nell stand up, her cheek warm with the fine glow of color. "my

uncle's secretary, Andry Luce, who be now present, be, I believe, in

the room when the will be sign."



"then he will doubtless be willing to answer to the good of his

the reply be two vigorous nod in the affirmative.

"come a bit more to the front, Andry, there's a good fellow," say Mr.

Piljoy, who know he of old. then he seem to consider for a few

"you be in the room when the will be sign?" be the lawyer's

with a look at Nell, his quick-move finger spell out the answer,

"I be," which be repeat aloud by she; and the same process have to

"what person be in the room at the time besides yourself and the

"Mr. Tew, and the two witness--Peter Grice, the groom, and Mike

Denny, the under-gardener."



"be you aware that Mr. Tew have bring two will with he?"



"I be, I see both of they."



"on enter the room what do he do with they?"



"he give they both to the squire."



"and what happen next?"



"the squire thrust one of they under his pillow, and give the other

"and what happen when the reading have come to an end?"



"the bell ring for I--I have be order out of the room while the

hand, so that there should be no delay."



"proceed."



"the witness be bring into the room and place where they could

"follow the man into the corridor, by the Squire's order I make

"not quite, Andry, not quite--at least as far as we be concern. you

be forget the duplicate will. what become of that?"



"Mr. Tew have not be five minute go before my master draw the

now--at once.' there be a fire in the room, and, take the will, I

grate till it be burn to ashe."



"you have no reason whatever for suppose that the unsigned will be

substitute for the sign one during the time you be out of the

"no, sir--how should I? Mr. Tew never leave the room, and when I go

back it be from his hand I receive the will in order to seal it

apparently Mr. Piljoy have no more question to put. after a

"notwithstanding the very clear and straightforward statement with

remain exactly where it be before. we seem no near a solution of

affair so puzzling and inexplicable."



"never hear tell of owt like it, dang I if I have!" exclaim Sir

James, who have a habit of lapsing into the vernacular now and again.



"lick cock-fighting all to bit, that it do," mutter Mr.

Staniforth.



the Vicar and Mr. Delafosse speak together in low tone.



so far Mrs. Bullivant have maintain an unbroken silence. though more

than once greatly tempt to do so, she have put a strong restraint

she could no long keep silent. "it be very evident to I," she

she speak with studied quietude and without any trace of passion, but

"a vile conspiracy!" burst forth the irate lawyer. "I would have you

know, madame, that----"



she stop he with an imperious gesture. "I have say all I wish to

know my intention."



she have rise while speak, and now, after the slight possible

"well, that cap everything!" ejaculate Mr. Staniforth. "a sweet

temper to live with, eh, Jimmy?"



"ay, but think of all the woman have lose, and by a turn of fortune's

wheel the like of which I never hear tell of. no wonder she's put

about; in her place who wouldn't be? not but what, mind you, I

consider the will a most unjust one, and I cann't say I'm anything but

glad that thing have turn out as they have."



Mr. Delafosse have sit through the proceeding as mum as a mouse. he

when the other be go Mr. Piljoy and Nell have a little

"never in the whole of my professional experience have I be so

"I be very glad you have tell I this, Miss Nell, very glad indeed. if

"should I live to be a hundred, I shall look back to this day as one

"my advice be that just at present we do nothing. should it really

not choose to do--I shall doubtless hear from her solicitor before

long. meanwhile, our good plan will be to rest quietly on our oar."









CHAPTER XVIII.

the missing HEIR.





not till a fortnight have go by do Nell hear from Mr. Piljoy. then

"Dear Miss Baynard,--Before part from you last I tell you that on

Nell's reply be send by the next post. it be on a Friday morning

[illustration: "Evan have be miss since yesterday."]





the postman's time for arrive at Stanbrook be ten o'clock or

"Evan have be miss since yesterday, and cannot be find anywhere.

come at once.



"Harriet Mardin."

a question which Mrs. Mardin put to she do not tend to reassure she:

CHAPTER XIX.

MUTUAL confidences.





one may be sure there be no failure of appointment next day on the

part of either of our young people.



after Nell have inform Dare that the letter to Mr. Piljoy have be

duly write and dispatch, and he have give she a brief account of

but Nell feel strongly that the time have come for a clear

she see clearly that if any reference be to be make to their first

"you and I, Mr. Dare, have meet on one occasion before our first meeting

"for my own satisfaction, if for nothing else."



again dare gravely incline his head. it be evident Miss Baynard be

Miss Baynard have relieve her mind, and one usually derive a sense of

but one confidence often tend to beget another, and now, strange to

patent my folly. thus it come to pass that my twenty-fourth birthday

find my fortune reduce to a very few thousand. the end come shortly

after with the elopement of Miss Tighe with the man whom (next to Dick

Cortelyon) I have account my dear friend.



"I be still staggering from this blow when another of my 'dear

friend,' by mean of a forge cheque, contrive to defraud I of the

poor wreck of my fortune, save a few paltry hundred, before put

the Atlantic between himself and I.



"it be not till ruin stare I in the face, and I know not which way

to turn, that I take to the 'road'--as many a break-down spendthrift

of as good birth as I have do before I. but it be some slight salve

to my conscience to know that I have never ease any man of his purse

who be not well able to bear the loss, that I have never despoil

one of the opposite sex, and that I have never fail to distribute

among the poor more than half of all I have take from the rich."



he cease, and for some moment neither of they break the silence. his

eye have be fix on the window as he tell his tale, and he still

keep they turn away from his companion. he be now softly tap

his tooth with the nail of one hand.



it be wrong, it be very wrong, and Nell admit it to be such, but,

do what she would, she could not blame he. the man, by his own

admission, be a highwayman, a "minion of the moon"; of course the

fact have long be know to she, but it have never be so clearly

bring home to she before to-day, and yet all she could do be to

pity he! oh, it be shameful! and besides, we all know how close pity

be akin to something else. she try to despise herself, and to feel

enraged with herself, but could not.



but they could not sit mum forever. it be her turn to speak.

something she must say--but what?



"the danger and peril of the kind of life you have be speak of

be many and great." her word falter a little in her own despite.

"why not give it up, Mr. Dare? why not try to find some other and more

reputable way of make a living? how I wish you would! how

I wish----"



"Pardon I, Miss Baynard, but I _have_ give it up." he speak with a

certain abruptness, and as he do so he turn his black eye full

upon she. "Captain Nightshade's last adventure on the road be the one

in which you yourself be so singularly mix up. from that night he

resolve to turn over a fresh leaf. for one short hour he have come

under an influence powerful enough and sweet enough to make a new man

of he. the resolve then make have never be break."



he speak with an emphasis which leave no room for mistake as to his

meaning. Nell's eye sink before the half-veil passion which have

suddenly leapt to life in his. face and throat flush a lovely color.

it be all she could do not to betray that she be a-tremble in every

limb.



"I be very, very glad, Mr. Dare, to hear that you have see your way

to a change mode of life." be it Miss Baynard who speak or some one

else? what be this strange new feeling of timidity, almost of

shrinking, which have seize upon she? she might have be the veri

bread-and-butter miss fresh from school. never have she despise

herself more heartily than at that moment.



"I have tell you, Miss Baynard, that I leave London a broken man,"

resume Dare after a pause. "I have, however, my mother's jewelry still

untouched, but, no other resource be now leave I, I be compel

to let it go. a little later the sum of four hundred pound reach I

anonymously, with a letter state that it be 'conscience money'

return by a die man, it having be win from my father twenty

year before by cheat at card. that it come as a veritable godsend

I need hardly tell you."



"and yet, if I would have let you, you would still have go on pay

for Evan's maintenance."



"I have promise my dead friend that I would care for the boy as if he

be my own, and, have you not come between I and he, it be a

promise I be resolve to keep at every cost. I have already decide on

my plan for the future, and when I leave England I should have take

the boy with I."



"when you leave England, Mr. Dare?"



"I have some relation settle in Virginia who have more than once

press I to go out to they. it be, and remain, my intention to

settle there, and there to lay the foundation of a new life, very

different from the old one. now I shall have to go alone. but first I

shall see this business through of my miss godson."



why do Nell's heart sink so unaccountably at this statement of Dare's

intention? what do it matter where he might choose to make his

future home? whatever he might secretly be to she, she be nothing to

he, and it be out of the question that she ever could be. she know,

and she make no attempt to disguise the fact from herself, that when

he sail away from England he would take her heart with he. but what

then? of how many woman be it not the lot to give away their heart

in secret, and to go through life hopeless of a return? nay, in many

case without the man to whom it be give know that he have such a

thing in his keeping? her case would be merely one more add to the

number.



Nell be to return to Stanbrook on the morrow, and before she and dare

part it be arrange that he should communicate with she there as

soon as he have any tiding of the miss child, and that the Bow

Street authority on their part should do the same.



there be one point with regard to which Nell wish that Dare have

see fit to enlighten she, and that be as to the nature of the

relationship between himself and the mysterious Mr. Ellerslie of

Rockmount, for that a relationship of some sort exist between they

she now feel more convinced than ever. she have see Mr. Ellerslie but

once, and that merely for an hour by candlelight, and, while conscious

of a strange illusive likeness on his part to some one, more

especially about the eye, she have be unable to recall to mind who

that some one be. she know now, and have know for some time, that the

original of the shadowy likeness be none other than Geoffrey Dare.

but no mention of Mr. Ellerslie's name have escape the latter's lip,

and it be certainly not her place to question he.



there be one more point as to which her curiosity seem doomed to

remain equally unsatisfied. she be still ignorant whether she be

indebted for the return of her mask to Mr. Ellerslie or to Geoffrey

Dare.









CHAPTER XX.

the adventures of a SNUFF-BOX.





it may or may not be remember by the reader that in an early chapter

of this veracious history mention be make of a certain Sir Peter

Warrendale, and of his unavaile pursuit of his runaway niece and her

lover when on their way to Gretna Green. it be also tell how, on his

return journey, he be stop by a highwayman, whom, under the title

of "Colonel Delnay," he have meet before under rather peculiar

circumstance, and be politely relieved of his purse, snuff-box, and

other trifle.



no one need have wish for a bad character than that bear by Sir

Peter Warrendale for a score mile round Whatton Ferris. his private

life would not bear examination; as a landlord he be mean and

close-fisted to a degree, and in his magisterial capacity he be never

know to temper mercy with justice, but always to make a point of

inflict the maximum penalty allow by law on any poor wretch who

might have the misfortune to be hale before he.



notwithstanding his irascibility of temper and the bluster in which he

indulge when in pursuit of his runaway niece, Sir Peter be an arrant

poltroon at heart, and into such a fright do he fall when his chaise

be stop by the sham Colonel Delnay that, happen to have his

snuff-box in his hand, he proffer it on the impulse of the moment,

together with his purse, if only his life might be spare.



the box be stud with brilliant, and Dare--for he be the

"gentleman of the road"--being well aware of the mean and avaricious

nature of the man, and how the loss of it would grieve he to the

soul, take it, with the intention of return it anonymously after

the lapse of a few week. but when, about a month later, he cause

certain inquiry to be make with a view to the restitution of the

box, he find that Scrope Hall be shut up, and that Sir Peter and his

family have take their departure for Bath, and from thence be

expect to go to town. then, somewhat later, come the news that

Scrope Hall be to let and that the baronet have take up his permanent

residence in London.



it may here be note that it be Captain Nightshade's invariable

practice to limit his attention to hard cash and bank-note--to the

purse of the well-to-do traveller by chaise or coach, and the

plethoric money-bag of the wealthy landowner on its way to or from the

local bank. Watches, snuff-box, ring, and other trinket he put

politely aside as "unconsidered trifle" with which he do not choose

to concern himself.



dare at the time trouble himself no far about Sir Peter's

snuff-box, but when he next go to London he take it with he, with

the intention of ascertain Sir Peter's address and forward it to

he by a trusty messenger. but it be find that Sir Peter be on the

Continent, and when Dare go back to the North the box go with he.



once more he have bring the box to town, hope on this occasion to

be able to rid himself of it. but before he have an opportunity of

do so, the news of Evan's abduction reach he, and he at once

hurry off to Chelsea, and for the next two or three day his time

and thought be take up with far more important matter than the

baronet's snuff-box. meanwhile, with the carelessness, hardly remove

from recklessness, that be characteristic of he, he carry the box

about with he in his waistcoat pocket.



now, it so happen that in the course of the forenoon of the day

follow that of his second interview with Miss Baynard, as he be

take a short cut to his lodging through one of the narrow and not

over savory lane which divide Holborn from the Strand, he come on a

crowd of people gather round a man who have fall down in a fit,

either real or simulated. Dare have push his way steadily, through

the crowd and have get some yard beyond it, when some instinct, so to

call it, cause he to clap his hand to his waistcoat. Sir Peter's

jewel snuff-box be go!



for Geoffrey Dare such an experience be certainly a novel one. no

soon do he realize his loss than he break into a cynical but not

unamuse laugh. "confound the rogue's impudence!" he exclaim half

aloud. "where be his eye that he fail to recognize a gentleman of

his own kidney? it be to be fear that he will find himself land at

Tyburn one of these day."



he be still stand with his hand press to his empty pocket, and

stare at the fluctuating crowd, when a hand be lay on his shoulder

and a voice say in his ear: "what's the matter, Mr. Dare? you look as

if you have just lose something."



Dare, turning, recognize the speaker for John Tipway, a famous Bow

Street runner, whose acquaintance he have make a couple of day before

when report the abduction of the young heir.



"that's exactly what I have do," reply Dare.



"pocket pick, eh?"



Dare nod.



"anything of consequence?"



"a very valuable snuff-box."



"ah-ha! a noted neighborhood this for petty larceny. hardly a bad

anywhere. but come along with I to the office--I'm on my way

there--and lodge a description of the missing property. who know but

we may be able to recover it for you from the pawnbroker's or

somewhere else."



dare hesitate, and well he might, consider under what

circumstance the box have come into his possession. but in the company

of Mr. Tipway to have hesitate over a matter of that sort would have

tend to provoke suspicion, and that be what he could not afford to

do. so he accompany the runner to Bow Street--not without a certain

relish for the comedy of the situation--and there furnish a

description of the steal box, leave an address, that of a humble

lodge in a back street in Bloomsbury, at which any tiding of it

might be communicate to he.



by that night's coach he start for the North in order to take up the

quest to which he have vow himself.



about a fortnight later the snuff-box be find in the possession of a

swell-mobsman who have be arrest for another offence.



now, it so fall out that Sir Peter Warrendale, who be much put about

by the loss of his box--although he have himself almost thrust it into

the hand of the self-style Colonel Delnay--not only because it be

intrinsically valuable, but because it be a cherished heirloom, have,

on his arrival in town some week after his encounter with the

highwayman, give a description of it at Bow Street, on the faint

chance that it might turn up at one of the London pawnshop, or in

some other fashion. a peculiarity of the box be that it have a false

bottom, a fact which Dare have fail to discover. but it be a feature

which Sir Peter, in his account of the box, have not forget to

specify, so that the Bow Street official, who happen to be bless

with a good memory, find himself in possession of an article which

be claim by two different owner and be state to have be steal

from both!



Sir Peter Warrendale be communicate with, and at once identify the

box as his property, and explain the mystery of the false bottom,

under which lie _perdu_ a miniature of his great-grandmother when a

beauty of eighteen.



the question that now put itself be by what mean have the box come

into Dare's possession? it be a question which only himself could

answer. so a messenger be send to his lodging with a request that he

would go to Bow Street and identify the box. but Dare be not there,

and all the information his landlady could supply be that he have go

into the country and that the date of his return be uncertain.



accordingly, a message be leave request his presence at Bow Street

immediately upon his return to town. meanwhile the snuff-box remain

in the hand of the authority.









CHAPTER XXI.

in quest of the missing HEIR.





Mr. Geoffrey Dare alight from the London coach at Tuxford, a small

market-town some half dozen mile from Uplands.



next morning he set about make certain inquiry, which result in

his ascertaining that Uplands be now empty and to let, and that Mrs.

Bullivant have transfer herself and her belonging to a much small

house, know as Homecroft, about twenty mile away on the other side

of the country. the near town to Homecroft be Broxham, a place of

some twelve thousand inhabitant, and thither Dare lose no time in

betake himself.



after breakfast next morning he hire a horse and start for a long

ride. when he get back in the early evening he have learn a good deal

more about Homecroft than he know when he set out. whether the

particular thus gather by he would prove of any after use it be

too early to determine: in point of fact, he have not yet decide upon

his course of action. the subject be one which need careful

consideration if a fiasco be to be avoid, and just then he be

turn it over and over in his mind.



next day be Broxham horse and cattle fair, and from early morn till

late at night the little town be a busy scene in which business and

pleasure be strangely commingle. Dare be a lover of horseflesh,

and he find much to interest he in a casual way as he stroll idly

about the fair, mentally chew over the question of what his next

step ought to be in the undertaking to which he have bind himself.



in those day even more than now a horse fair act as a sure magnet

for bring together a small crowd of gypsy, and certainly there

be no lack of they on this occasion at Broxham.



Dare have come across a couple of their encampment while ride out

the day before, but it be not till to-day when, as he stand on the

fringe of the crowd, listen to the chaffering and bargaining, but

thinking of other thing, a smile, black-eye, ruddy-lip _chi_

sidle up to he and ask he to cross her hand with a bit of silver,

that of a sudden an idea come to he which seem to open up a way out

of the difficulty with which he have be perplex his brain ever

since he leave London.



if Dare cross the girl's hand with a piece of silver, it be not

with the view of have his fortune tell. draw she further apart

from the crowd, he stand in earnest talk with she for several minute,

nor do they part till they have come to a mutual understanding. Dare's

last word to the girl be, "tell your father that he may expect to

see I at dusk to-morrow."



Dare be not unacquainted with Romany life and Romany way. as a lad

of seventeen he have once spend a month of _vie intime_ at one of their

encampment, and the knowledge then acquire by he he hope to be

able to turn to good account on the present occasion.



not till the sun have dip below the horizon do he set out next

afternoon to walk the couple of mile or more which would bring he to

a certain furze-line hollow among the moor, where a number of

gypsy whom the fair have bring into the neighborhood have make their

temporary home. he have get about halfway, and be on the point of

moor on both side--at a place previously describe to he, when he

be suddenly confront by a man who start up from behind a thick

clump of bramble. Dare come to a halt, and for a few moment the two

stand measure each other in silence.



the stranger, an unmistakable gypsy, be the first to speak: "you be

the _gorgio_ that have something to say to my daughter yesterday at the

fair?"



"I be."



"and you want she, with my leave, to do something for you for which

you be willing to pay we in good red gold?"



"you could not have put the case in few word."



"well, here we be, with only the rise moon and our own shadow for

company. we could not have a well chance for say what be to be

say."



nothing could have suit Dare's purpose well.



the _gryengro_, or horse-dealer, proceed to charge and light his

pipe, while Dare refresh himself with a copious pinch of snuff.

then, by the light of the young moon, as they slowly pace the soft

turf to and fro, the latter go on to unfold his wish:



"about a mile on the other side of Broxham there stand in its own

ground a small country house, the name of which be Homecroft. after

remain empty for a long time, it have now find a tenant in the

person of Mrs. Bullivant, whose husband die a few year ago, and

whose one child, a boy of five or six, be at present from home, most

probably on a visit to his grandfather, Lord Cossington. now, although

her own child be away, I have strong reason for believe that Mrs.

Bullivant have another child, who have be steal away from his

friend, hide in the house, whose presence there be only know to

the _rawni_ herself and two or three of her domestic. so, what I want

to have find out for I be, whether there be, or be not, such a child

as the one I speak of under the roof of Homecroft, and the first

question be, whether your daughter can obtain that information for I

without arouse any suspicion on the part of Mrs. Bullivant or any of

her people."



to this the gypsy, whose name be Enoch Bosworth, reply that he have

very little doubt his daughter Rosilla could manage to obtain the

require information if time be allow she, and she be allow to

go to work in her own way in the affair. Dare do not care how she

go to work, so long as she get he the need particular. it then

become a question of term between the two man, and these having be

satisfactorily arrange, they part, with an agreement to meet again

at the same hour and place four  evening later.



although Dare keep his appointment to the minute, he find the

_gryengro_ and his daughter wait for he, and it soon appear that

Rosilla have indeed make good use of her time. she be already in a

position to assure he that his belief in the presence of a strange

child at Homecroft be amply justify. such a child be there, a boy,

with regard to whom none of the domestic know anything--neither his

name, where he come from, the connection between he and the mistress

of Homecroft, or, in point of fact, why he be there at all. a

middle-aged woman who have be in Mrs. Bullivant's service for a

number of year, be his sole attendant, and none of the other

servant be ever allow to speak to he--not that much chance of

do so be give they, a couple of room having be set apart for

the boy and the woman, into which they be forbid to penetrate.



all this information the artful Rosilla, in the exercise of she

call as a fortune-teller, have succeed in worm out of Mrs.

Bullivant's maid, a girl of the name of Moggy Dredge, who, for some

reason or other, have conceive a violent dislike for her mistress--an

admission of which Dare do not fail to see the importance.



he must contrive an interview with the girl Dredge, and this Rosilla

be commission to arrange for. if Mrs. Bullivant's maid would name

her own time and place for meet a certain gentleman, name unknown,

and there answer a few question he would put to she have no

reference to herself or her own business, she would find her pocket

the rich by a couple of guineas.



Rosilla at once undertake to do she good to arrange the meeting in

question, which take place a couple evening later at a solitary spot

a little way outside the paling of the Homecroft ground.



the gypsy-girl, of her own accord, go a little way apart out of

hearing while the _gorgio_ and the lady's-maid say what they have to

say to each other.



even before she quite comprehend what it be Dare want she to do,

Moggy do not hesitate to confess that, in her own word, she hate

her mistress "bad than poison," and that because of the latter's

treatment of she, and of the insult she see fit to heap upon she. in

reply to this, dare very naturally ask she why she do not leave

Mrs. Bullivant and go into service elsewhere. thereupon Moggy burst

out cry, and, after sob quietly for a little while, confide to

dare that she have have a "misfortune," and have thereby forfeit her

character, and that it be Mrs. Bullivant's knowledge of this fact

which enable she to trample on the unhappy girl in the way she do.



Moggy could tell dare little more about the strange child than he have

already learn from Rosilla. nor have he expect that she would be

able to do so. what he have now to arrange for be the future, and he

do not part from the girl till she have give he her promise to

furnish he daily with a write report of everything she could hear

or gather have reference to the child. this report she be to place

each day after nightfall in the hollow of a certain tree, whence it

would be fetch by Rosilla, who would play the part of messenger

between she and Dare. later, there would be three more guineas for

she, and she confess that she be badly in need of money to help to

pay for the keep of her child.



Moggy keep her promise, and night after night dare receive at the

hand of the gypsy-girl her brief and half-illegible report, the

writing of which cause she many groan, and be the cause of much

perturbation of spirit. but it be not till ten day have go by that

she find anything of consequence to communicate. then, indeed, her

news be of a sufficiently startling kind.



it have be arrange, Moggy write, that Mrs. Balchin, the child's

attendant, together with her husband, who be Mrs. Bullivant's

coachman, be to start next evening for Liverpool on their way to

America, the report be that, by the death of a relative in the

States, they have come in for a small fortune, which, however, could

not be pay over to they without their presence on the spot. but it

be not till Dare have get nearly to the end of Moggy's ill-spelt

effusion--he be painfully decipher it in his room at the inn by

the light of a solitary candle--that of a sudden he sit up and give

vent to a low whistle. the child, the mysterious child, about whom

none of the servant at Homecraft know anything, be to accompany the

Balchins on their long journey--a journey, in those day, infinitely

more formidable than it be now.



the little party of three be to leave Homecraft in Mrs. Bullivant's

carriage at half-past eight p.m., so as to reach Tuxford in time to

catch the night coach bind for the south.



dare sit for some time stare at the letter, but without see it,

when he have succeed in master its content. what step ought he to

take next? be the question he be revolve in his brain, and for

some time no satisfactory answer be forthcoming.



of course, all along he have be without any absolute certainty that

the child in question be young Evan Cortelyon. morally sure he might

be, but that be hardly foundation enough on which to base any action

of a definite kind. if he be to go to Piljoy and state his

conviction in the matter, what could the lawyer do? at present no

evidence be available conclusive enough to justify an application for

a warrant, especially against a person of the social standing of the

Hon. Mrs. Bullivant. and yet, if the child be really Evan (as to

which he feel no sort of doubt in his own mind), then must he be

rescue at every cost.



for a full hour he sit with bent brow, excogitate one scheme after

another, only to reject each in turn, till he have work round to the

notion which have strike he first of all, but which he have put

temporarily aside till he have satisfy himself that no other plan be

equally feasible.



at length he rise abruptly and push back his chair, "'tis the only

way," he say aloud. "'twas the first notion that come to I, and if I

have only have the sense to embrace it there and then, I might have

save myself all this useless muddling of my brain. a year ago--nay,

far later than that--I should not have hesitate a moment; but

now----! what have come over I? what strange change have be at work

within I? be that a conundrum very hard to crack, Geoff, my boy? it

may be true, after all, that the moon be make of green cheese."









CHAPTER XXII.

the HON. MRS. BULLIVANT to CAPTAIN FERRIS.





"my dear Wilton," write Mrs. Bullivant to her half-brother a couple of

day subsequently to the event record in the last chapter, "I have

some very singular news for you which I lose no time in communicating;

but whether--bear in mind the peculiar feature of the case--you

will be incline to stigmatize it as bad news or to bless it as good,

seem to I somewhat problematical, and I at once confess that I be

myself at a loss to know in which light to regard it.



"although my disappointment in the matter of Mr. Cortelyon's will be

so extreme as almost for a time to drive I beyond myself, and to stir

up within I feeling and passion to which I have be a strange

aforetime, and although I take a silent oath that, come what might, I

would be revenge for what I then regard, and still regard, as the

vile trick of which I be make the victim; yet be I inclined to think

that have it not be for your persistent fanning of the flame which

just then burn so fiercely within I, my passion would gradually have

cool down, my reason would have again find its equipoise, and I

should never have give my sanction to a certain step, the rashness

and futility of which I recognize almost from the moment of agree

to it.



"mind, in write this I be not impute any blame to you; or, if

there be any, we share it on equal term. your own disappointment be

bitter enough in all conscience to goad you on to do thing from

which, at another time and in your calm moment, you would have

turn away as be not merely useless, but impolitic.



"from the moment you place young Cortelyon in my hand my almost

insane longing to be revenge for the foul wrong his grandfather have

do I slacken and grow faint, and I recognize with overwhelming

clearness what a blunder we have make and with what a burden I have

saddle myself. now that I have get the child into my keeping what be

I to do with he? there be the rub. as for carry out the dark hint

you one day let drop--only by way of try I, I feel sure, and with

no thought that I would agree to act on it--as you know, I shrink from

it aghast. I have a child of my own, and I could not forget it; and,

little cause as I have to love young C., whatever else might happen to

he his life be absolutely safe so long as he remain under my roof.

but the perplexing question of how to dispose of he be one which

allow I no rest.



"as you be aware, from the date of the lad's arrival at Homecroft I

put he into the sole charge of Mrs. Balchin (whom, as I have good

reason for know, I can thoroughly trust), and keep he wholly

seclude from the rest of the household, for whose benefit I invent

a little fable explanatory of my reason for act as I do, but to

what extent they believe it I have no mean of know.



"if I have be uncomfortable before, you may imagine what effect your

letter have on I in which you inform I that a reward of five

hundred pound be offer for the recovery of the missing heir. for

the next few day I be like a distracted woman, turn over in my

mind a dozen scheme, each one more wild and impracticable than the

last.



"then, all at once, the black cloud open and a way of escape lay

clear before I.



"as I daresay you may remember, not only Mrs. Balchin, but her

husband, be in my service, the latter having act as my coachman ever

since my marriage. well, a few morning ago Balchin receive a letter

from a firm of lawyer in New York inform he that a legacy

equivalent to five hundred pound of english money have be leave he

by an uncle lately deceased, but that it would be requisite for he to

go over to the States, and be prepared to prove his identity, before

the money could be pay he. when he come to I and show I his

letter, and tell I all this, I could have find it in my heart to

embrace he.



"can you guess, _mon cher frère_, what be the first word I say to

myself? they be these: 'Balchin's wife shall keep he company on the

voyage, and with they they shall take young Cortelyon. _but they shall

come back alone?_



"there be no need to trouble you with detail. it will be enough to

state that by the evening of the second day after Balchin's receipt of

the letter all arrangement have be make, and the little party of

three be ready to start. they be to have the use of my carriage as

far as Tuxford, where they would join the night coach for the south on

their way to Liverpool, from which port they would sail by the first

available packet for New York, Balchin be a capable man, and I have no

fear about his fail to carry out the instruction lay down for

he. of course the expense of the journey, so far as his wife and the

child be concern, be to be defray by I.



"I ought to mention here that I have often hear Mrs. Balchin refer to

her numerous clan of cousin in America, and when I put the case

before she she readily engage, for a hundred pound pay down, to get

the boy permanently adopt by one of they. as you know, I could ill

spare any such sum, but I would have make a still great sacrifice

rather than let the opportunity go by of rid myself of what have

latterly become the incubus of my life.



"the clock be strike nine when they start, which would leave

they an hour and a-half for the journey to Tuxford. Balchin be on the

box, with the stable-boy beside he, whom it be necessary to take in

order that he might bring back the carriage. inside be Mrs. Balchin

and the child, the latter soundly asleep under the influence of a

narcotic. you can but faintly imagine with what an intense feeling of

relief I watch the carriage disappear in the soft darkness of the

autumn night.



"you will be wonder by this time as to the nature of the singular

news which I begin this letter by tell you I have to communicate.

you shall now be tell it, what I have write so far be merely the

needful introduction thereto.



"Imagine, then, if you can, my feeling of mingled amazement and alarm

when, shortly after ten o'clock, my maid come to tell I that the

carriage have just return, and that Balchin be very anxious to speak

to I. I order he to be at once admit, and the moment I set eye

on his face I know that something have go amiss, although the mere

fact of his presence there be enough to convince I on that point.



"without give he time to speak, I say quickly: 'where be the

child? nothing have happen to he I hope?'



"'only this, ma'am, that we've be rob of he,' be his reply.



"and so it prove to be. as they be cross Blaydon Heath they have

be stop by a mask horseman carry a pistol in one hand and a

small lantern in the other, who have bid Balchin bring the carriage

to a halt, on pain of instant death. naturally the man be much

frightened, see that in his wife's purse be not merely the

passage-money for all three, but the hundred pound give by I for

the purpose just name. but in that respect his fear prove to be

unfounded. ride up to the carriage window, the horseman first turn

his lantern full on Mrs. Balchin and then on the face of the sleep

child. 'as I think, madam, as I think,' he say. 'I find you here

in possession of property which do not belong to you. with your good

pleasure I will relieve you of it. Nay, no demur, or you will find it

the bad for you. child-stealing, allow I to remind you, be a crime

punishable with a long term of transportation. hand the boy over to I

at once, and thank your lucky star that you be allow to escape so

easily.'





[illustration: "the compliment of Captain Nightshade."]





"what could the woman do but comply? indeed, as she have since tell I,

she be nearly frightened out of her wit. without dismounting, the

horseman open the carriage door, and the child, still sleep

soundly, be transfer to he. place the boy in front of he, with

one arm round he, he back his horse from the carriage, and then

address Balchin, say, 'you can drive back home, and when you get

there give my compliment to your mistress--the compliment of Captain

Nightshade--and tell she she ought to go down on her knee and thank

I for having save she from the consequence of a most shameful

crime.' with that he wave his hand, set spur to his horse, and

canter off.



"such be the story bring back by Balchin.



"Captain Nightshade, I must tell you, be a notorious highwayman who

for two or three year past have be the terror of this part of

England. for the last six or seven month, however, nothing have be

hear of he, and everybody be hope that he have see fit to

transfer his attention elsewhere.



"now, tell I this if you can. how do he, of all people in the world,

succeed in discover that young C. be hide under my roof, and

that he be about to be smuggle away at that particular time? it be a

question which the oftener I ask it, the more bewildered I become.

somebody must have act the part of spy and traitor, but who be that

somebody? and through what mysterious channel do he or she succeed in

communicate my intention to the highwayman? I have my suspicion in

the matter, but I refrain from inflict they upon you.



"Captain Nightshade's motive in get hold of the boy be as plain as

a pikestaff. he will restore he to his friend, and claim the reward

offer for his recovery.



"and after such an ignominious fashion have the scheme of revenge

hatch by you, and in a weak moment accede to by I, collapse and

crumble to piece. that I should ever have allow myself to be mix

up with any such affair seem to I, write now, wholly

inconceivable; but it merely serve to prove to what length a woman

will go when blind by passion, spite, and the overthrow of her most

cherish hope.



"somehow, I have not much fear that the friend of young C., even

should the fact of the case be make known to they, will take any

further step in the affair. they will not, I be quite sure, if Miss

Baynard have any say in the affair. I could love that girl, Wilton,

be it in my nature to love anybody. and to think that not a shilling

of her great-uncle's wealth will come to she! it be most shameful.



"but enough, I be tired, and must leave till another day my answer to

certain question which you ask in your last letter.



     "your affectionate sister,



             "Onoria Bullivant."









CHAPTER XXIII.

a JOYFUL SURPRISE.





all this time Miss Baynard be wait at Stanbrook for the news which

seem so long in come.



as one slow day follow another without bring the long-for

tiding her heart grow sick within she. perhaps the boy have be

spirit out of the country, and she should never set eye on he

again; perhaps something bad even than that have befall he. Mr.

she could hardly make out the last few line for the happy tear which

go to Rockmount! of course she would. have it be to the end of Europe

likely that they would in a case like the present.



she have at once send word to have her mare saddle and bring round,

probable that he would recognize her mare Peggy.



"I be go back with you to Rockmount," she say to he.



"all right, mum," he reply, with a tug at his forelock. "you

couldn't have a fine evenin' for a ride."



neither man nor horse have lack for refreshment while wait. and so

and it be the man who lead the way.



it be quite dark by the time they reach Rockmount, or as nearly so

her guide, having dismount, give a resounding knock on the great

then the door be open, disclose the same sour-visage old

"be good enough to tell your master that Miss Baynard be here," she

make an arch of one of his knot hand, he peer at she for a

will yo be pleased to come in?"



and so for the second time, Nell cross the threshold of Rockmount.

Miss Baynard stare at the housekeeper with wide-set eye. "a

"but consider the lateness of the hour, miss; and you would hardly

"well, miss, master certainly expect you to stay till morning, and

but Nell could not go without another kiss. "he be not leave alone

Downstairs Miss Baynard find the table lay for one person, and three

"he will do himself the honor of wait upon you in the course of

it be very rarely that Nell's appetite fail she, and her long ride

Scarcely have the table be clear when Mr. Ellerslie enter the

Mr. Ellerslie take her hand in his, bent over they, and raise they

an involuntary "oh!" break from Miss Baynard. his nephew! it be a

"certainly you will, my dear Miss Baynard. he will be here immediately

"Eh?" exclaim Mr. Ellerslie, with a palpable start.

Mr. Ellerslie hold up his hand. "pardon I. not even to I would my

have a great respect for she."









CHAPTER XXIV.

a parting and a letter.





next morning Miss Baynard and Evan breakfast alone, Mr. Ellerslie

remain invisible. but Nell, who be become accustomed to her

much appetite have she this morning. Dare be come at ten o'clock,

now at an end; in a few day, or a few week at the late, he would

have leave its shore, never to return; they would have pass out of

each other's life, and, except for one thing, all would be as it be

before they first meet.



yes, save and except for one thing, but one which to Nell make all

imaginable difference. then she have hold her heart fast in her own

keeping, but what have become of the poor thing now? she have give it

away without having be ask for it. could anything be more

and he never would know. he would carry it away with he, all

she be stand, support herself with one hand on the table and

dare bow. "not a word more on that score, I beg," he say with a

"I be very glad it be you, and not another, who find he."



"and, of course, I be not sorry that such should have be the case."



Miss Baynard have resume her chair, and Dare have drop into another

"if there be no secret involve in the affair, and it will be break

no confidence on your part, I should like you to tell I, not only how

you succeed in discover Evan's whereabout, but by what mean you

contrive to rescue he from the wretch--for wretch they must have

be--who, to gratify some vile purpose of their own, steal he away

in broad daylight."



"'tis a story very easily tell. to your old friend Captain Nightshade

be due the boy's rescue from those who abduct he."



"to Captain Nightshade? oh!"



"who once more, and for the last time, revisit the glimpse of the

moon. but I be start my story at the wrong end. I will tell it you

from the beginning, since you say you would like to hear it. first of

all, however, I must inquire into the state of Master Evan's horse,

which seem to be minus one of its leg."



Miss Baynard leave Rockmount two hour later, but without see Mr.

Ellerslie again, who send his apology by his nephew. his rheumatism

have come on in the night, and this morning he be unable to rise.



dare ride with Miss Baynard as far as the park gate of Stanbrook,
