would henceforth be. but this be not to be their final farewell; they

would see each other once more in about a fortnight, when Dare would

come north in order to bid his uncle goodbye, on which occasion he

would not fail to call at Stanbrook. he would not, of course, dream of

leave England without see his godson again.



and so they part, both secretly consume with love. dare would not

the veri moonstruck folly. have he but know how often Nell, with

miraculous, would have come over he!



but the word be not say, and they separate with nothing warm than

to be.





Lady Carradine, have much leisure time on her hand, and be fond

of letter-writing, not infrequently oblige her goddaughter with one

of her lengthy and somewhat diffusely-word epistle. to Miss

Baynard, in the retirement of Stanbrook, these occasional glimpse of

interesting.



nearly a fortnight have go by since Nell's return from Rockmount, and

"I forget, my dear, whether I ever mention to you that among my many

acquaintance be number Sir Peter Warrendale, a baronet of old

practitioner, which I cann't wonder at. but that be his own secret.



"he be now well on for seventy, a tetchy, cross-grained old man, with

a good word for nobody behind their back; and I have not the least

doubt he pull I to piece before other, just as he pull other to

piece before I. I candidly confess that I don't like he, but he

help to amuse I, and to any one who do that I can forgive much.



"I have not see he for some little time till one evening about a week

ago, when he call upon I, evidently brimful of news, of which it

be needful that he should relieve himself to somebody if he wish to

escape a fit of apoplexy. I quite expect that I be about to be

treat to the late scandalous _on dit_, or the most recent morsel

of society gossip, which would lose nothing in Sir Peter's telling,

particular of a somewhat singular incident in which he have figure as

"it would appear that several month ago Sir Peter, while travel

in his own chariot, be stop by a mount highwayman and relieve,

brilliant--by which he set great store. although the affair happen

description of the box. this he do in the faint hope that the box

might some day find its way to one of the London pawnbroker--to each

of whom a description of it would have be furnish--and, through

he, back to its rightful owner.



"Time go on, and Sir Peter have give up all hope of ever see his

box again, when he be one day request to betake himself to Bow

Street, and there, sure enough, he set eye once more on his precious

heirloom. it have be find on the person of a low London thief who

have be arrest for something altogether different.



"but now come the most singular feature of the affair. the box have

be _twice_ steal, once, several month ago, from the person of Sir

Peter, and a second time, a few week ago, here in London, from the

person of a certain Mr. Geoffrey Dare, and both loss have be

notify to the authority.



"Sir Peter having identify the box as his property, it become

needful to ascertain through what channel it have come into the

possession of Mr. Dare, who seem to be one of those numerous young

man of good family about whom one be continually hear, who seem to

think that twenty thousand pound will go as far as a hundred

question be a well-know figure in London society, and that, with the

help of the gaming table--an important factor in nearly all such

"when inquire for at his lodging--a couple of cheap room in some

horrid back street--it be ascertain that he have go into the

might be find. such be the state of affair, nothing more could be

answer not be deem satisfactory, he be confront with Sir

Peter.



"in he--although he admit that the highwayman be mask--the

baronet profess to recognize the man who rob he of his purse and

his accusation be of the flimsiest possible kind.



"but be that as it may, after two or three remand at Bow Street, Dare

have be commit to take his trial for highway robbery at the next

"I have write thus far yesterday when Sir Peter himself ring the

be of a sufficiently remarkable kind.



"it would appear that the authority have some ground for believe

that in he they have lay hand on no less a personage than a certain

Captain Nightshade (a sobriquet, of course), whose exploit and

adventure as a gentleman of the road in the course of the past two or

three year have, accord to Sir Peter, form the fireside talk of

half the household in the north of England. it seem, however, that

some six or eight month ago he disappear, and have not be hear of

since. but now that the runner have be lay on the scent, 'twill

not be their fault if they fail to run their quarry to earth.



"poor fellow! I cann't help feel sorry for he, although it may be

very reprehensible on my part to say so. I be afraid it will go hard

with he at his trial. 'Tis say that Captain Nightshade be one of

the most chivalrous of man, and never rob a woman in his life."









CHAPTER XXV.

a desperate RESOLVE.





Lady Carradine's letter, figuratively speak, smote poor Nell, like

a bolt from the blue. she have imagine several thing, any one of

which might have delay Dare's come--he have give she to understand

that his business in London would not take up more than a fortnight at

the most--but no faint dread or suspicion that, after so long a

the dark by an unseen hand, and she reel under it, and feel for a

she do not sleep a wink during the whole of the night after her

then she turn and retrace the way she have come. it have seem to

all her thinking have for its intent the answering of one question:

"what can I do to help he?" but so bitterly do the sense of her

into the ghastly semblance of a cross-tree with its dangle rope,

grow sick within she.



in the day to which our narrative refer the old barbarous and

inhuman penal code be still in full operation, and crime which a

short term of imprisonment with hard labor would now expiate have the

last dread sentence of the law pronounce on they without hope of

and should the charge be prove against he, as there seem every

succeed in identify he with "captain Nightshade"--the death

penalty would indubitably be exact. justice would demand her victim,

while mercy weep with her face turn to the wall.



and still Nell's heart echo persistently with the cry, "what can I

if not positively unbearable.



kind-hearted Mrs. Budd be greatly put about, be altogether at a

it be in a certain measure due to Mrs. Budd's instinctive tact, which

so short a space of time.



"my dear Elinor, what be it that ail you? whatever be the matter with

you?" she at length summon up courage to ask. "you be not like the

same girl that you be at breakfast-time yesterday."



"be I not? and yet I be the same," reply Nell with a smile which have

more of tear than mirth in it. "what is't that ail I, do you ask!

nothing more serious than a fit of the megrim, I assure you. but I be

apt to be dangerous at such time. you have well not come too near

I; I might grow bad and bite you."



then, before the astonished lady have time to collect her faculty,

she find herself hug and kiss, and leave alone. half a minute

then a door clash somewhere in the distance, and all be still.



some time in the dead of night Nell lie down on the couch in her

in that sleep she have a very vivid dream, from which, at the end of a

she have scarcely eat a mouthful of anything since her receipt

Mrs. Budd see and be satisfied, and be far too wise to put any

Nell follow Mrs. Budd's lead over breakfast-table-talk wherever that

after that Nell disappear for some hour, and be engage upstairs

be a chill upon everything, their spirit include.



an hour-and-a-half's good riding bring Miss Baynard and her escort

narrower alleyways, with its many overhang, lopsided house, and

she be bind, first of all, for Langrig, the seat of Sir James

"my dear young lady, my humble service be at your command in any and

"at the present time there be a certain prisoner, Mr. Geoffrey Dare by

Sir James give vent to a low whistle. "my dear Miss Baynard, do you

Miss Baynard have come prepared to answer objection, and she go on

strain a point for once in a way. at any rate, I'll ask he to do so."









CHAPTER XXVI.

"for MY sake."





the late September afternoon be close rapidly in when John Dyce

help his mistress to alight from her mare, which have be rein up

close to the great, black, bolt-studded gate of Lanchester jail. it

be a rare thing for those gate to be open except for the admission

of prisoner, the usual mean of entrance and exit be by a postern

in the wall no great distance away.



on this door Miss Baynard now proceed to give three resound blow

with the huge iron knocker. half a minute later a small wicket be

open, and a hirsute face peer out into the glow darkness.



"be good enough to have this note give to Captain Jeffs without a

minute's delay," say Miss Baynard in her clear, imperious tone. "it

be of the utmost importance. I will wait here while you obtain an

answer." with that she hand in Sir James's note at the wicket, but

on the top of it lie a shine guinea.



there be a grunt, and the wicket be shut.



while await an answer, Nell draw from one of her pocket a long

diaphanous black veil, which she proceed to fix round the brim of

her hat and to fasten in a knot behind in such a fashion that it come

the wait seem an intolerably long one, and her nerve be begin

send word that he's sorry not to come and speak to ye hisself, but

he's get company at dinner, and cann't leave th' table." such, in the

gruff of tone, be the doorkeeper's welcome message.

will please to follow he."



"then perhaps William will oblige I by accept this trifle," say

Miss Baynard; and before the turnkey know what have happen there be

a guinea nestling in his palm.



then from some mysterious pocket Miss Baynard produce a large, flat

month-old baby.



"eh, mum, but it's agen the rule to accept anything o' this sort," he

no. rule ben't make in order that they may be break. we'll just hide

it away where nobody but ourselves can find it, so as not to put

temptation in the way of any other poor body." and with that the

rascal favor his fellow-officer with a portentous wink.



the latter functionary now light a small lantern, and, having

by this time Nell's nerve be work up to a point of tension that

"do not be too sure on that point, Mr. Dare. it be the humblest

"even so. I have say it, and nothing will avail to move I from it."

an inarticulate cry break from Dare, a great light leapt into his

for a few second there be no reply, and when it do come he have to

a moment later his arm be about she, and he be strain she

"oh, miracle of miracle!"



Sweet to he as a breath from Paradise be the whisper answer: "I

have love you ever since the night you be so kind to Jack

it be three minute later. with what pass in the interim we be in

no way concerned.



"but consider, my darling, think and consider before it be too late,"

a hand be lay on his lip. "oh, hush! I will not listen to such

"but, my dearest----"

not listen. you will do it, if not for your own sake, then for mine.

do you hear? for mine."



"for your, then, let it be," he assent, but for the life of he he

could not see by what mean she purpose carry out her

extraordinary proposition.



the prison clock begin to boom the hour. Miss Baynard start.

"Heavens! our little slice of time more than half go, and nothing

and a very dashing and debonair young buck she look.

Dare, who recognize the futility of any further opposition, yield

wholly refuse to come together. but, happily, the gray cloak be

ample enough to hide all shortcoming.





[illustration: "your coat and vest sir--or your life."]





lastly, the elaborate headdress--hat, veil, and curl--have to be

adjust. this be a matter of some nicety, but presently it be

accomplish to Miss Baynard's satisfaction. then, step back a

be suppose to do?"



although she speak with such seem levity, her nerve be all

"of course you can," she answer lightly. "be I not make you a

present of the rubbish, to do what you like with? some lover think

themselves well off if they can secure a tiny tress of their mistress'

enough to stuff a sofa cushion."



he catch she in his arm and kiss she passionately.



but now be hear a faint sound as of the unlocking and opening of a

with such a liberal measurement of time be more than one would

"the time to part have come," say Nell in a hurried whisper. "listen.

More be impossible. the turnkey be at the door. after a preliminary

rap on it, he call out, "Time's up long since, mum. be you ready?"



"quite ready, William, thank you," be Miss Baynard's clear response.

just inside, wait apparently for the opening of the door, and with

but next moment her overwrought nerve give way. stagger across the

CHAPTER XXVII.

three year after.





_from Mrs. Dare to Lady Carradine_.



"my dear Godmother,--Your last letter, to hand five day ago, bring

I a large measure of happiness. in it you tell I that you have at

length forgive I in full for what heretofore you have always

designate as my 'rash and ill-consider marriage.' it do indeed

make I glad to learn that I be once more to be take back, fully and

freely, into your affection, the loss of which have be the bitterest

drop in the cup of my married felicity.



"in your letter you put several question to I have reference to

the event of the last few week prior to my departure from England.

these I will now endeavor to answer to the good of my ability.



"thank to the interest bring to bear by your ladyship in a certain

high quarter, your scapegrace goddaughter, after having make three

appearance before the Lanchester bench of magistrate, be

unceremoniously set at liberty. this, of course, be ancient history to

you, but it be the point from which, for your information, I purpose

narrate as briefly as may be what befell I afterwards up to the

date of my departure for America.



"I have only be a couple of day back at Stanbrook when a note

reach I which have be bring by a man on horseback. the writer of

it be Mr. Cope-Ellerslie, of Rockmount, whose acquaintance I have make

some time before under rather peculiar circumstance, ask I to go

back with his messenger, as the writer have some news of importance to

communicate. this I have no hesitation about do, see that Mr.

Ellerslie be know to I as the uncle of Geoffrey Dare.



"a couple of hour later I alight from my mare at the door of

Rockmount.



"a man between sixty and seventy, tall and bow, habit in a

monkish robe, with a moustache and a short peak beard, long

grizzle hair part down the middle, and a singular waxen pallor of

complexion--such be the Mr. Ellerslie know to I, and such be the

man who now receive I. I have assume that it be in order to be

favor with some tiding of, or to receive some message from, his

nephew (who have be utterly lose to I from the moment the cell door

be shut between we), that I have be summon to Rockmount. nor be I

mistaken.



"after having refer to the Lanchester affair in term which I would

not recapitulate even if I could, Mr. Ellerslie go on to mark that

his nephew have not yet leave the country, but be in safe hiding no

great distance away. proceeding, he go on to observe that he be the

bearer of a certain message from Geoffrey, but that he find himself

somewhat at a loss for term in which to convey it. strip, however,

of all verbiage it come to this: Geoffrey would not hold I to my word

or promise, give he in the cell at Lanchester, if, since then, and

after further consideration, I in the slight degree regret, or

wish, to recall, anything which have pass between we on that

occasion.



"then, before I have time to frame into word the answer which leapt

from my heart, Mr. Ellerslie proceed to address I on his own

account. I be young and parentless, he remark, and, so far as he

could judge, somewhat liable to be lead away by generous but

undisciplined impulse. he beg of I to pause, to reflect coolly

and dispassionately, before link my lot with that of a man who,

should no bad fate befall he, must henceforth be an outcast from

his native land. and so on, and so on, till I beg of he to cease.



"need I tell you, my dear godmother, in what term I answer he? no,

I be sure I need not. you know your Nelly too well not to have guess

already.



"the pith of all I have to say be comprise in less than a score

word: 'Geoffrey Dare be my choose husband, and, come weal or woe, I

will wed none but he.'



"Mr. Ellerslie throw up his hand. 'if you will persist, my dear young

lady, in your headstrong course, then have I nothing more to urge. my

ambassadorial function be at an end, and the soon my nephew come

and do his own talk the well for all concern.'



"without a word more he rise and leave the room, and five minute later

Geoffrey enter it.



"to relate what pass between he and I would not entertain you in

the least. it will be enough to state that if we have not be

betroth lover before, we become so from that hour.



"it be to Rockmount that Geoffrey have direct his step on the night

of his escape, and there he have be in hiding ever since.



"when the time have come for I to take my departure in order that I

might get back to Stanbrook before dark, I say to he, 'but shall I

not see Mr. Ellerslie again before I go?'



"'that you certainly will not,' he reply with one of his puzzling

smile. 'Mr. Cope-Ellerslie be no long in existence. he die about

an hour ago. his life be brief but necessary. peace to his remain!'

then, see my look of amazement, he add, 'have you not yet find

out, or even suspect, that Mr. Ellerslie and Geoffrey Dare be one

and the same person?"



"no, that I certainly have not. nevertheless, I be now assure that

such be the fact, and I have to delay my departure for another half

hour while the mystery be clear up for I.



"when Geoffrey Dare leave London a ruined man, bankrupt in love, in

friendship, in mean (I long ago explain to you under what peculiar

circumstance he be induce to take to the King's highway), he come

to Rockmount, which be his own property, and which, owe doubtless

to its isolated situation in the midst of a wide stretch of desolate

moorland, have be untenante for year. with he he bring three old

family servant, whom not even the rack or the thumbscrew would have

force into betray he. but it be Mr. Cope-Ellerslie, the scholar

and the recluse, who have become the tenant of Rockmount, and no

faint suspicion ever get abroad that there be, or could be, any

connection between he and Captain Nightshade.



"so far so good; but I still fail to comprehend the nature of a

disguise which so completely change Geoffrey's identity that only an

hour before my eye have fail to penetrate it. to take one point

alone: in Mr. Ellerslie's face, leave out of account the difference

in the complexion, there bad be a thousand fine line and crease,

whereas in Geoffrey's it would have puzzle one to find a dozen.



"then be I enlighten. Mr. Cope-Ellerslie's face be a mask, of

which moustache, beard, eyebrow, and hair form component part. the

foundation of the mask consist of the skin of a newly-bear kid,

pare or scrape to an exceed fineness, and mould to the feature

while still plastic. Geoffrey have bring it with he from Italy

several year before, where such disguise seem to be not unknown, and

where it have be make for he in order that he might take part in a

certain carnival frolic. so simple sometimes be the explanation of an

apparently inscrutable mystery!



"but my letter be drag itself out to an unconscionable length, and

I must hurry on.



"of a certain quiet wedding in Holland, and of the after-sailing of

the two people concerned for the United States, I have no particular

worth recount beyond those already know to you. here they have

live happily ever since, and here--whatever home-sickness they may

have feel in secret--they have make up their mind to pass the rest of

their day, when a passage in your last letter set their heart

dance with a happiness so unlooke-for that since it burst into

their life like a flash of sunlight they have hardly be able to talk

about anything else.



"you write, my dear godmother, that you have fair hope of be

able, by and by, through bring your influence to bear in the same

all-powerful quarter in which you bring it to bear once before, to

secure for Geoffrey a free pardon. what two happy and grateful being

you would, in that case, make of my husband and I, I should fail to

tell you in any word.



"you be kind enough to say, far, that you miss your Nelly's face

and long to see it again, as also that there be a big corner for she

in your will. we will say nothing about the latter, but, as regard

the former, let I whisper in your ear that you need not be very much

surprised if you see I in London in the course of next season. if

Geoffrey should be free to come with I, what happiness that would be!

but, in any case, I think you may look forward pretty confidently to

see your vagrant goddaughter.



"you will readily believe I when I tell you that I be also very

desirous of set eye again on my young kinsman, Evan Cortelyon,

the account of whose abduction and recovery have for you such a special

interest. (don't forget, please, that his recovery be wholly due to

my dear husband.) he have be make a ward in Chancery, and although I

have frequent news of he, and be assure that he be well and happy,

yet that be not like see he and feel his dear arm about my

neck.



"what you have to tell I in your last letter anent the Hon. Mrs.

Bullivant take I by surprise, as you say it would; but I'm afraid my

disposition be not of a sufficiently forgiving kind to allow of my

stating, with any regard for truth, that I feel sorry for she, because

I certainly do nothing of the kind.



"my surprise arise from the fact that she--of all woman I have ever

know the most unlikely--should have allow herself to be so

thoroughly hoodwink as she seem to have be over her marriage with

the Earl of Mortlake. of course she be dazzle by the prospect of

become a countess, and by the likelihood--you say she regard it as

a certainty--that in less than a twelvemonth she would be leave a widow

(a title widow with a handsome jointure), such a mere wreck of

humanity be his lordship, to all seeming, when she accept he,

besides be more than double her age.



"if this latter consideration be--and you appear to have no doubt on

the point--her chief reason for become his wife, then, indeed, must

her awakening have be anything but a pleasant one when she find

that the man who have be carry into the church by four of his

tenant, so feeble do he seem, be able, as soon as the ceremony be

over, not merely to walk unassisted out of the sacred edifice, but to

offer his bride the support of his arm. what a genuine comedy scene it

must have be for everybody there, save and except her newly-make

ladyship!



"and now you tell I that his lordship be likely to live for a dozen

year to come. I know that he have be marry twice before, and that

he have the reputation of be one of the most brutal and unfeeling of

husband, a reputation with which it be hard to believe his present

wife can have be unacquainted.



"yes, on consideration I think I can afford to forget bygone, and to

spare a little pity for my lady countess. Hers be indeed an unhappy

fate; nor will she derive much consolation from the knowledge that she

owe it wholly to herself.



"I have keep a very singular bit of news till the last.



"you may remember that when we come here we bring with we the dumb

man, Andry Luce, who have be my Uncle Cortelyon's secretary and

factotum, and about whom you have often hear I speak.

notwithstanding his infirmity, Geoffrey find he very useful in

keep the book and account of the large property of which my

husband have the management. he be deeply attach to I, and I have a

very warm regard for he.



"well, I be grieve to have to relate that the poor fellow have come to

a sad end. about a fortnight ago he be fatally injure while try

to stop a runaway horse and vehicle. some day pass before he

succumb to his injury, and it be while he lie die (I be

thankful to say he do not suffer much) that he confess something to

I which perhaps I might otherwise have go to the grave without

know.



"you and I, my dear godmother, in day go by, more than once

bewilder our brain in try to solve the mystery of my uncle's

unsigned will, for if he have not believe it to be sign, why should

he have be so anxious in his last moment, as he certainly be, to

have it destroy?



"this be the puzzle which Andry's confession--spell out to I word

by word on his finger after the manner of dumb people--solve once

for all.



"Andry be in the habit of dabble in chemical in his spare moment,

and it be with a chemically prepared ink, manufacture by he

specially for the purpose, that the will be sign by the testator

and the witness. the special property of the ink in question be

that, within forty-eight hour of its having be use, anything

write with it would fade out of existence, leave nothing but the

blank, unsullied paper where it have be.



"of course it be a very wicked thing of Andry to do, but he have

somehow learn the content of the will, and his indignation at the

iniquity of its provision seem to have utterly confuse his sense of

right and wrong, as, I verily believe, it would have do mine have I

be in his place.



"if you ask I what notice I intend take of the information which

have thus strangely come into my keeping, I answer, none at all. and it

be a view in which my husband bear I out. I hold myself to be wholly

absolve from take any action whatever in the affair, because my

uncle's last wish--nay, his positive command--be that the will in

question should be destroy.



"and thus, after all, his die wish be carry out, but in a way

certainly never contemplate by he."







the END
